Coffee with Crow: Building A Future Where Everyone Can Work with AI | ASU+GSV Summit 2026

Global Silicon Valley(14.5K) ·42:16 ·2 views · 2026-04-17
Gina Raimondo (Former U.S. Secretary of Commerce) Michael M. Crow (President, Arizona State University)
To prevent a repeat of the "China shock" displacement of the 1980s, the U.S.
TL;DR To prevent a repeat of the "China shock" displacement of the 1980s, the U.S. must establish a "grand bargain" where employers and universities co-design a continuous, "K to grey" learning system that prioritizes individual empowerment over shareholder-only gains. ...

TL;DR

To prevent a repeat of the "China shock" displacement of the 1980s, the U.S. must establish a "grand bargain" where employers and universities co-design a continuous, "K to grey" learning system that prioritizes individual empowerment over shareholder-only gains.

The "Grand Bargain" for Workforce Security

  • Addressing Public Anxiety: Gina Raimondo notes that over 70% of Americans fear AI-driven job loss. She warns that failing to address this anxiety with a concrete plan will lead to political over-regulation that stifles U.S. competitiveness.
  • The Employer Mandate: Companies must transition from being passive consumers of talent to active drivers of the education system.
      • Skill Definition: Employers must continuously define the skills needed for today and tomorrow.
      • Bridging the Gap: Unlike the manufacturing shift to China, which left millions without a path to the service economy, the AI transition requires a "bridge" for workers of all ages to pivot.
  • Continuous Learning: Education can no longer be a "one-and-done" event; it must be a lifetime of continuous upskilling to match the pace of technological change.

Transforming Higher Education: The "CEO" Model

  • Institutional Inertia: Sethuraman Panchanathan argues that universities are currently too slow and remain in denial, often settling for "adaptation" when "transformation" is required.
  • The Five Pillars of Co-Creation: Panchanathan proposes a "CEO" mindset (Collaboration, Innovation, and Transformation) based on:
      • Co-creation & Co-design: Developing curriculum alongside industry leaders.
      • Co-habitation: Eliminating the "four walls" of the university to embed faculty and students within real-world industry environments.
      • Co-evolution: Updating the roles of tenure-track faculty to include practitioners and "professors of practice" who live in the real world.
  • Democratizing Opportunity: While talent is universal, opportunity is not; universities must leverage AI to scale access to elite learning resources for the "massive" population rather than just the "best and brightest."

AI as a Tool for Student Success

  • The Three Competencies: Marie Lynn Miranda outlines three pillars every student must possess to survive the AI era:
      • Fluency: The ability to incorporate AI into sophisticated process workflows.
      • Criticality: Understanding the limitations of Large Language Models (LLMs), specifically how they miss information on the "tails" of distribution and can be manipulated.
      • Ethics: Knowing when to disclose AI use and identifying inherent biases in data models.
  • AI-Informed Data Science: The University of Illinois Chicago uses data science to proactively support first-generation students.
      • Predictive Support: By analyzing non-cognitive assets (e.g., financial stability, support networks) and academic history, the university provides tutoring *before* a student fails a midterm.
      • Eliminating Rote Work: AI is used to handle administrative data entry for advisors, freeing them to focus on high-impact, one-on-one human transformation.

Principled Innovation and the "Agentic Self"

  • Individual Empowerment: Michael Crow argues that the "tech-bro" narrative of a "Death Star" AI replacing humans is heartless. Instead, the focus should be on creating an "agentic self"—using AI as a personal enabler to help individuals make better decisions and project their ideas.
  • The Moral Imperative: Advancing science without a democratic purpose is viewed as an amoral act.
      • Liberty to Learn: Technology should be used to advance the "individual liberty" of the worker and the success of the family unit.
      • The Veil of Ignorance: Crow suggests a new philosophy where AI provides "unlimited equal opportunity" by acting as a personalized learning assistant for every human being, regardless of their starting point in life.

Notable Quotes

"If AI creates America's first trillionaire... and millions of the rest of us are left behind, that's not a success. That's horrific for a democracy; it certainly makes us less competitive." — Gina Raimondo
"We have to reverse the logic that the 'tech bros' are talking about—this sort of 'Death Star' image of a tool that's going to replace everyone. If we do this right, each person is going to be individually and personally empowered." — Michael M. Crow
"Entry-level jobs will still exist. The requirements for those entry-level jobs are changing significantly, and universities have an obligation to make sure we’re equipping people with the skills they need." — Marie Lynn Miranda
"Universities today are too slow and they are in denial... What is required is a mindset shift or a paradigm shift. It can be done, it should be done, and it must be done." — Sethuraman Panchanathan
Good morning, everyone. So good to be here. Quick show of hands, who made it to karaoke last night? A couple of you. Okay. So we're segueing from last night's karaoke to a lyric: "The times, they are a-changin'". That's what we're talking about here today. What do these changes mean for our workforce and how will universities evolve? Let's jump in with questions for our panelists. Gina, I'm going to start with you this morning. You served as the US Secretary of Commerce in the Biden administration and as the 75th Governor of Rhode Island. In March, you wrote an essay in the New York Times about workforce displacement, and you warned that you fear that we might see the kind of displacement that your own father experienced in Rhode Island in the eighties. You call for a new grand bargain between governments and employers. What would that kind of bargain in a best-case scenario deliver for workers? Yeah, good morning. It's fabulous to be here. I was just saying to Michael, I came to this a dozen years ago, and it was so much smaller. It's an incredible credit to his leadership that how much you have flourished, and to all of you for the work you're doing. So Michael, thank you for having me and thank you all for what you do. I think we're in a tough spot right now as it relates to AI. I spent my career, especially as Commerce Secretary, focused on US competitiveness. How does the United States compete and out-compete in the world? And I think AI is a key piece of that. We need to lead the global competition in AI. But right now, 70 plus percent of Americans are afraid of AI. When they hear AI, they hear, "I'm going to lose my job." You know how you know it's bad? I was at a bar a couple weeks ago watching NCAA basketball, and all everybody was chattering, "What's your post-AI job?" "I'm going to be a park ranger," someone said. "I can't be thrown out because of AI." Another person was saying like, "God, I'm paying all this money for college tuition for my kids. What are they going to do when they leave?" So we got to... you know, Americans are anxious, and we have to honor the anxiety, not just with empathy, but with a plan and with action. Because otherwise, politicians are going to over-regulate AI, stop it, and that's not competitive. So what's a grand bargain mean? A grand bargain means companies have to really, finally step up and be key drivers in our entire education system, our entire workforce training system. An effective workforce training system, which I would argue is not one that we have in America, would have companies continuously defining what are the skills needed today, what are the skills needed tomorrow, and then universities, workforce training partners, other workforce initiatives can continuously fulfill those needs. And it can't be one-and-done, right? You can't go to school once and think you're fine forever. It has to be continuous learning so that, in the grand bargain, in the AI economy, an American has confidence to know, as the labor market changes, as necessary skills change, there's a chance for them in that economy so that they can continue to change. You mentioned my father. Last thing I'll say is in the China shock, you know, we sent all our manufacturing to China. People had their blind... companies had their blinders on. It was more profitable and more efficient. And guys like my dad, millions of them, just got left behind. Well, he was 56. He needed a job and there was no bridge to a job in the services economy for him. And that's what I mean by a grand bargain. Be pro-AI, be pro-innovation, but man, figure out a bridge so people can continuously learn and have a place in a constantly changing economy. Marie Lynn, you're the chancellor of the University of Illinois Chicago. You know something about bridge building; you do a lot of that for your students. What's one move that you recommend that higher ed leaders make in the next year to meaningfully improve how universities connect learning and work? Yeah, thank you. And thank you for including me in this conversation. And I just want to forewarn all the people in the front row that I talk a lot with my hands. I have a handheld mic. Be prepared to catch it. You're in the danger zone. It's like the splash zone at an aquarium. I'm readjusting in case. Pardon me? I'm just getting ready. So this is all outlined in an opinion piece that I just published in Science Magazine. So I think that higher education has an absolute obligation to make sure that this technological wave of innovation actually includes the full swath of the population. We've had many technological waves. Those waves have created incredible economic growth and prosperity, but they've produced it in a very uneven way where certain people who had access to capital and networks, and early access to the technology, they benefited enormously and huge swaths of the population got left behind. With AI, we have an opportunity to do things differently. And I think that higher... institutions of higher education, especially ones like Arizona State and University of Illinois Chicago that serve so many Pell Grant students, first-gen students, students who don't have those sorts of networks and capital assets at their disposal, we have the obligation to do something different. So we, in conversation with employers, talking to employers about what they need, what they want in their hires, in their interns, etc., we've developed this idea that there are three things that we're going to make sure that every one of our students, undergrad, professional student, graduate student, has at their disposal vis-a-vis AI. That is, they will be fluent, critical, and ethical users of the technology. By fluent, I mean able to use the technology, able to incorporate it into process workflows, to be able to use it in a pretty sophisticated way. By critical, I mean not that everybody needs to be able to program large language models, but people need to understand the basics of how they work. Large language models draw their results from the middle of the distribution, so information on the tails of the distribution is not represented very well. So if we had AI in the era of "should we wash our hands" before seeing the next patient, AI would have said no, because there was very little evidence and that was all on the tails of the distribution. And it's also true that the distribution can be manipulated for political or economic purposes. So people need to be critical users so they can evaluate the output. And they need to be ethical users. They have to figure out when to use AI, when not to use AI, when to disclose the manner in which AI was used, when to incorporate it into process workflows, and pay attention to whether or not that is going to create some... if you incorporate it into a particular process workflow, will it incorporate some natural biases that are part and parcel of large language models? And do you actually want to do that? So we're committed to making sure that all of our students graduate with those capabilities. Those capabilities come very much from our conversations with employers. So I'm a hundred percent behind Secretary Raimondo's idea that higher education and companies, employers, need to be talking to each other robustly. It's something we've always done at UIC and I know it's done a lot at Arizona State, at least under your leadership, Michael. Yes. Michael, turning to you. So when there's a displaced worker who's looking to get upskilled, how might they turn to a university and what do universities need to do to evolve so that continuous lifelong learning is the standard? Well, one of the things that we need to do is we need to realize that we need lots of different kinds of universities. We need universities of multiple ways of working. And so the university that we've been building at ASU is one in which we decided no boundary barrier. So that any learner from any stage of learning in their life, any teacher, any family, any child, anyone K-12 could have access to the assets or the tools that the university might be building. And so the key to all of this, and I liked Marie Lynn's example of this way to teach AI tools to the college students, couldn't be more important. When you think about the fact that at some point we have to reverse the logic that the tech bros and some of these other guys are talking about—this sort of Death Star image of this unbelievable powerful tool that's going to alter everyone, everything, and replace everyone in work. I think in the long run if we can figure out how to do this, each person's going to be individually and particularly and personally empowered. If the tools can be designed in a way where they help the individual to learn, help the individual to make decisions, and help the individual to project themselves. If you can build this idea or this concept of an agentic self, and you can have universities then find a way to help facilitate that, help make that happen, help role model that with the right kinds of ethical uses and controls and so forth and so on, then what Gina is focused on in terms of trying to figure out how to rather than go through disruptions, we go through enhancements for the first time. In terms of these kinds of technologies, it means that the universities have got to then rethink their role, at least some of them. It can't be just, "Well, we're producing the best and the brightest to do this and this and that." You know, that got us to where we are. That got us to this point of very significant internal national social and economic friction within our country because we've made education something which is attainable in a limited way as opposed to a massive way. We've made education something that is accessible to some but not enough. We've made these tools for advanced learning something that become this big massive disruptor rather than this personal enabler. And so what we need are universities that are much more adaptable, engageable, flexible in addition to, I'm not saying in lieu of, but I'm saying in addition to other kinds of places. And so what I'm arguing for are places in which some of the universities with their assets become deeply embedded at the level of the communities, at the level of working with these companies, working with the workers, working with the transition, developing these kinds of tools. One of the sessions after this, I think the next one, we have Will.i.am who's our partner who's built this course for a hundred or so people in California and Arizona where they're building agentic selves. Well, we're learning a lot in the building of the agentic self. And this agentic self is coming out of the university, coming out of the design and the tools that the university has, the pedagogies that we have, but it's now for the first time focused on the building of one of these massive technological capabilities down to the level of the individual. So a lot of the vision of AI is at the macro level. It's going to have macro level impacts, but micro level empowerment if we think about it correctly, which is where we really need to put our energy. Panch, as the 15th director of the U.S. National Science Foundation, you've thought about these macro and micro level impacts. I think, you know, first I want to say that being a researcher in AI for the last 35, 40 years, it's exciting to see where we are right now but it's also interesting to see this because people think that somehow this all happened magically. And I'm trying to make the case here, because I can't but help use this platform to underscore the importance of fundamental discovery research investments. And here we are today because of those sustained investments, even in AI winters over the last five to six decades, and that's what we're seeing right now. So I want to make that point first, because we have to understand that. I don't think we appreciate this enough; even in this audience we don't appreciate this enough, I think. The second point I want to make is that I find—and I'm just going to say this and I'm going to offend a lot of people probably in this process—I find that universities today are too slow and they are in denial and they at best adopt and adapt, which is in my view as a mathematician, insufficient. Maybe it is sufficient, but you know, but insufficient; maybe it is, you know, necessary but not sufficient. I fundamentally believe that we need a completely different look. And what is that "it"? That "it" is "i" innovation and "t" transformation. It's a completely different way of looking at the world. And Gina talked about the industry, and so I'm going to sort of frame it... I always like to frame it in some kind of a thing, you know, sort of with letters. So I'm going to use the word CEO. I think we have come to a point where we really need the CEO of collaboration first. Partner, partner, partner first. And so there should not be any distinction between even within the university the disciplines—we have talked about that enough these days in universities—but with other sectors, industry being one of them, of course. There are other sectors more than industry; all of them need to play a role in this. So it's that CEO. And that collaboration then leads to co-creation. I think we need to co-create. Somehow thinking that it can be done in the university, as fond as I am myself being a tenured faculty member, I think it is not sufficient. I think for us to think somehow we're going to create it out of the university just as faculty members is not sufficient. It's not going to work. So it's a different world. So co-creation and then co-design, co-delivery of the curriculum, for which we need co-habitation. It cannot be where you have a university with four walls and you think somehow it's all going to happen there because somebody from industry is going to come and partner with you. That is insufficient in my view. So the co-habitation idea is something that we need to really welcome and embrace. So it's a different world. And if we do the work with co-habitation then what happens is co-evolve. You co-evolve and that's how it's going to be; the co-evolution is going to happen with not just only regular faculty but all kinds of faculty even in the university environment. Professors of practice, teaching faculty, tenure-track faculty, research faculty, practitioner folks that are in industry and other folks who live in the real world outside of the university. All of them have to play a role in this as co-creators. It cannot be just only us. So I think this is something that I'm really keen to build. And what can we do for AI, with AI, by AI? Yes. But what is x plus AI or AI plus x through that? And what does it look like? And that's what we need, are these completely transformed individuals. I think we need to make it possible. And I think it's new models, new ways in which we do this, new delivery processes. I think it's not just four years, six years in the university; it's all the way from K to grey. So it's got to be a complete spectrum of people that we need to get involved in this right from day one. So I think it's a very different mindset that we all need to possess. And this audience being one where we have always looked ahead of the curve, 17 years having been in the very first one and also the one that Gina mentioned. And I was prohibited in the last five years to participate, so it was with agony that I watched from outside the things that you do. "Those were the best meetings, those last five years." No, no! But I had the good fortune of working with my dear friend Gina Raimondo and I did a lot of things in Chips and Science. What Commerce and NSF did, through what we did in terms of the 27 AI institutes that we launched all across the country. And I want to just stop with this. The AI institutes would be... when we launched it, I said that it's got to be completely partner, partner, partner. Five AI institutes were fully paid by USDA, believe it or not. Five institutes of agriculture. It's in context, so go look at all the 27 institutes and the regional innovation engine programs with AI, you will see the transformation starting to happen. Because, to what Marie said, talent and ideas are democratized, opportunities are not. So it's a very poignant moment for us to see what we can do with what AI can do. Panch, thank you. It's good to have you back. Marie Lynn, partner, partner, partner, co-habitate, co-create. What would it look like in Chicago to do that? You and I talked a little bit about how AI empowered with student data could be really meaningful for your students, especially first-generation students. How are you starting to envision what it might look like to leverage AI to enable student success? Yeah, I'll give you a specific example. Before I do that I just want to riff off some of the things that were said. You know, one of the things I'm really tired of hearing is entry-level jobs are going to be eliminated. Because that takes responsibility off the universities and it makes the students feel depressed. Entry-level jobs will still exist. The requirements for those entry-level jobs are changing significantly, and the universities have an obligation to our students and our K-12 system has an obligation to students and apprenticeship programs. All of that have an obligation to make sure that we're equipping people with the skills that they need. So we have a deep AI intervention, if you will, in... I'll give this example, in our advising system where we have a platform that all of their data from high school pours into. If they submitted test scores of any type, those get poured into it. Anything from their time at the university gets poured into it. And the other thing that we do is it's referred to as a non-cognitive assets survey. Everybody who joins UIC has to take this non-cognitive asset survey before they can sign up for their first set of classes. And those ask questions like: "If your car broke down and it was going to cost $300 to fix it, would you be able to get it fixed?" "If you were sick and you needed to go to the doctor, is there someone you could call who would take you to the doctor?" Those non-cognitive assets tell us so much about the kinds of supports that students need. So, the non-cognitive asset survey, their high school grades, their grades from the university, their test scores, any notes from any advisor who's spoken with them at all can all be put onto this electronic platform. And then we use data science to crunch through this information on a regular basis to identify what the match is between what we believe the students need and the assets that we have at the university. So we're matching that up and rolling that out to students before they come in and say "I think I'm having problems in my calculus class." So I can look at your high school background in math, I can look and see that you're an engineer, and I can say "I think it's a really good idea that you get started in the tutoring center for your calculus class from week one" instead of waiting until you have problems with your first midterm. So this is an all... I don't know whether I would call it AI or AI-informed data science system. I know there are... you and I can have a discussion about whether it's data science and AI is part of it or AI and data science is part of it. I think I'm a data scientist, so I think it's data science and AI is part of it. But you know, so there's this very proactive investment in understanding the context of the students' lives: if they're commuting students, if they're first-gen, all those sorts of things. And it has substantially changed our retention rates for students and has decreased the time to graduation. It's increased our graduation rates, and incorporated into all of that is where are they working on campus, are they developing some of their professional skills there, where are they interning, where are they looking for jobs. Because we're interested in getting students to and through college and then placed into a good job. So it's a deep and proactive intervention. It seems to be working; the students like it, the advisors like it because they spend less time entering data and analyzing it and more time one-on-one with the students, which was the reason they became advisors in the first place. So that's one of the things that I say about AI: we can use AI to do this rote work that really isn't why people went into education, to free people's time up to do the work that they did go into education for, which is to interact with people to help transform their lives through the educational process. Thank you. Michael, a question for you, then I'll be coming to Gina. ASU has advanced the idea of practicing principled innovation. How should that framework shape the way that universities adopt and leverage AI, especially when the pressure to move quickly is so intense? Well, we have to stop listening to people who are anti-democratic, who do not believe in liberty, that believe that these machines are going to become the masters of us all. And we need to start focusing on the individual and the empowerment of their individual liberty—that liberty to learn, liberty to advance, liberty to move their families forward, liberty to express their ideas. And so what I mean by that—and I apologize for going so far back to a root—if we're practicing innovation in which we become... and you can see these people talking about these technologies all the time, they are heartless. They are not interested in the success of the family, they're not interested in the success of the democracy. Some of them aren't even interested in the success of the country. They're interested in the success of their investors, and there's nothing wrong with that as an objective. I'm stating this in somewhat harsh terms because it's been unbelievable to me the extent to which there has not been a focus on the empowerment of the person. So the principled innovation objective that we have in all of this is the empowerment of the child, the success of the family, the success of the worker, the advancement of the person to an opportunity where they have more opportunity rather than less opportunity. I literally have had people come up to me saying, "Well you're just going to have to get ready because you're going to be wiped out." And I'm like, either you're a psycho or a fool. I don't know which one you are. You're probably just a greedy little bastard. And so we have to bring some principles about the success of our idea: the individual, liberty-empowered democracy which is built around the individual and their life and their family and their progress. The more we focus on that, the better we will end up at the end of the day. It's not simple; it's very difficult with these macro tools that are available to everyone. We have people running around saying, you know, "Universities are worthless" and "Universities are not important" and "Don't send your kid." We have some of these jokers that are funding people not to go to college on purpose thinking that it's some kind of a game. So they find some genius in the eighth grade and they empower that kid to do something. Oh, well good, good. I'm glad you empowered that person to do something. There's another few billion of us who aren't these geniuses in eighth grade; we have to find a way to make our family work. And so what I'm interested in, in terms of principled innovation, is how do we advance with the notion of these core values, these core structures of what it is that we're trying to build and how do we use our technologies to achieve those things. And so there was this philosopher that I probably read like too many philosophers, but this one I probably read almost every word he's ever written, Philip Kitcher. And the best book that he wrote is "Science, Truth, and Democracy", in which he outlines that in a democracy as you advance science and technological knowledge, if you do not do it to achieve the purposes of the democracy you are carrying out an amoral act which leads to immoral outcomes. Now that's heavy stuff for nine o'clock in the morning. And so principled innovation means: what are we building all these things for? We're building it for the success of the person, the achievement of their life, and so that's what we need to focus on. Thank you. Gina, turning to you, what does it look like for employers to practice principled innovation? No clapping. What does it look like for employers to practice principled innovation? Yes, so much to unpack in what Michael just said and I agree with it. You know, that assumes the people you're talking about have a desire to be moral actors. Some don't. Exactly. You know, that's not the goal, right? I mean I often say, if AI creates America's first trillionaire and a couple of the first trillion-dollar IPOs and millions of the rest of us are left behind, that's not a success. That's horrific for a democracy; it certainly makes us less competitive, not more competitive. And to really get to the issues that you're talking about, the political system in which we're making these decisions needs massive change. And that's the thing I worry about, by the way. Unless we make the changes necessary to empower the average individual, the average company, the average startup, new company, and instead just focus on creating trillionaires and trillion-dollar companies, this will not end well. There are people including me worried about the race with China for AI. The picture I just described may well land America in a place where we have the best designed chips, the most innovative AI companies, the most sophisticated models—and still be losing the global AI race. And that's our challenge, right? You have to say what does it really mean, what will it really take for America to lead the global AI competition? And it is much more than just plentiful energy, plentiful chips, plentiful data centers. It's the stuff we're talking about. And what does it mean for employers? Most... I agree with you, it's exciting to think about what a well-trained individual can do with AI capability. How much more productive they'll be, how much more creative they'll be. Emphasis on well-trained. I actually think we'll see an explosion of new company creation if we do it right. So what's the role of a company? Companies need to really understand that they need to be in the driver's seat with respect to our future. The future is theirs to create. If they simply sit back and conceive of their job as maximizing short-term shareholder value, I think this will wind up very poorly. In fact, it is in their interest to make sure our education system works—what do you say? I don't like your analogy because I'm never going grey—from... it works for everyone from birth to death of all geographies, ages, stages, etc. This isn't a business versus worker problem. This is an everybody problem and they better get in the driver's seat and start driving along with the rest of us. By the way, last time I checked agents don't walk into the store and buy things, people do. How is deep recession, social upheaval, a frayed democracy good for a business? Answer: it's not. So what do they need to do? Yeah, keep the pedal to the metal on innovation, for sure. But also keep the pedal to the metal on people strategies so every American is brought along. And don't think that that's somebody else's problem. Don't think that's the government's problem or a university president's problem. No, it's your problem too. Yeah so one way to focus on that—I agree with both these comments—is to stop viewing human capital as just a commodity and start viewing them as human beings. And the second you start thinking about human beings as being this particular part of the economy and everyone including the companies has responsibility for empowerment of these people, then you calculate the net return. The net benefit to the entire system of doing that, it'll be a measurable return if we stop acting in such selfish narrow interests. And so human capital has got to be seen as the work product of all the companies, all the corporations, all of the government agencies. It's about human capital development; it's about the empowerment of the individual human. If we could get that worked into the model, then we would find a way to, rather than as opposed to... let's abandon these workers as we move our factories overseas in Detroit and Philadelphia and all these other places and then wonder why the people are mad or upset. Well they're mad or upset because they got attracted to be a worker there and then the company said "Well, you know, we found some cheaper workers somewhere else, too bad for you, you should move there." That shouldn't be the way that the world works. Everyone, if they'd been working to build that individual to the highest adaptability level, everyone would win including those corporations when they come back later and say "Where's all the workers? I can't find anybody that's qualified to work for me." Well of course you can't, because you abandoned the person to focus on your company, not the person. Okay show of hands in the audience, we're going to do up, side, or down. At this moment in time, are you feeling hopeful about this discussion that we're having right now? Are we on the right track? LIVE: President Trump gives Tax Day remarks, participates in a roundtable — 4/16/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·50:00 ·2.5K views · 2026-04-17

President Trump highlighted the impact of the 2025 "great big beautiful" tax bill, which has led to record-high tax refunds for workers through the elimination of taxes on tips, overtime, and Social Security, while incentivizing domestic manufacturing through auto-loan deductions and tariffs.
TL;DR President Trump highlighted the impact of the 2025 "great big beautiful" tax bill, which has led to record-high tax refunds for workers through the elimination of taxes on tips, overtime, and Social Security, while incentivizing domestic manufacturing through auto-loan...

TL;DR

President Trump highlighted the impact of the 2025 "great big beautiful" tax bill, which has led to record-high tax refunds for workers through the elimination of taxes on tips, overtime, and Social Security, while incentivizing domestic manufacturing through auto-loan deductions and tariffs.

Key Tax Reforms and Financial Relief

  • Tax-Free Income Categories: The administration highlighted three major pillars of the recent tax legislation aimed at working families and seniors:
      • No Tax on Tips: Specifically targeted at hospitality hubs like Las Vegas; workers reported refunds ranging from $5,000 to $8,000.
      • No Tax on Overtime: Claimed by more than 26 million Americans to date.
      • No Tax on Social Security: Over 51 million seniors now pay zero federal tax on their Social Security income.
  • Average Refunds: Secretary of the Treasury Scott Bessent reported that the average refund this season is over $4,000, exceeding initial administration expectations.
  • Estate and Small Business Relief: The bill effectively eliminated the "death tax" (inheritance tax) for small businesses and farms to prevent heirs from having to mortgage or sell properties to pay tax bills.
  • Withholding Adjustments: Secretary Bessent urged workers to update their withholding levels to see an immediate "surge" in take-home pay rather than waiting for annual refunds.

New Savings Initiatives and Deductions

  • Trump Accounts: In partnership with Michael and Susan Dell (who contributed over $6 billion), the government has launched "Trump Accounts" to build generational wealth.
      • Newborn Seeding: The Treasury seeds these accounts with a $1,000 contribution for every child born during the term; 5 million accounts have already been opened.
      • Growth Potential: The initiative aims to provide children with up to $100,000 by the time they reach adulthood.
  • Auto Loan Interest Deduction: A new provision allows Americans to deduct the interest paid on car loans, provided the vehicle is American-made.
      • Over one million Americans have already utilized this deduction, seeing an average write-off of over $2,000.

Domestic Manufacturing and Energy

  • Trade and Tariffs: The President emphasized the use of tariffs to encourage foreign companies to build plants within the U.S., specifically noting a massive influx of pharmaceutical and automotive manufacturing.
  • Energy Production: The U.S. currently produces more oil than Russia and Saudi Arabia combined, with projections to double that lead by 2027.
  • Stock Market Performance: The Dow Jones Industrial Average recently reached the 50,000-point milestone.

Social Policy and Community Impact

  • Education Freedom: The tax bill expanded school choice and made home-schooling expenditures tax-deductible via federal tax credit scholarships.
  • Women’s Sports: Trump reiterated his administration’s stance against allowing biological men to participate in women’s sports, characterizing it as a top-tier priority for protecting opportunities for female athletes.
  • Public Safety: The President claimed the lowest murder rate since 1900 and highlighted the elimination of taxes on overtime for first responders to support local law enforcement.

Geopolitical and Military Updates

  • Conflict with Iran: The President reported significant military success in the ongoing conflict, claiming the destruction of 158 Iranian ships and the elimination of key terrorist leaders.
  • Military Technology: Trump touted the effectiveness of U.S. missile defense systems, noting a recent engagement where 111 incoming rockets were successfully intercepted.

***

Notable Quotes

"This is the birthplace of a little idea known as 'No Tax on Tips.'... It was not an expensive consultant. A young waitress came up to me during dinner and she said, 'Sir, we should have no tax on tips.' I said, 'That’s the greatest thing I’ve ever heard.'" — President Donald J. Trump
"Under President Trump, we are celebrating how much more money hard-working Americans will keep, not how much the government will take. Within 24 months, President Trump's idea became the law of the land." — Scott Bessent, Secretary of the Treasury
"With the passage of the great big beautiful bill, my wife and I have benefited from the elimination of taxes on overtime, allowing us to stretch every dollar in an increasingly expensive world. With less financial stress, I'm able to be a better and effective asset to my community." — Cruz Littlefield, Las Vegas Metropolitan Police Officer
"In Nevada, we have what is called a tip compliance... the IRS comes in and they say, 'You're going to make this amount of tips... and we're going to tax you on that even if you don't make that amount.' So to see [no tax on tips] come to fruition was amazing. It is promises made, promises kept." — Nicole Williams, Culinary Worker

The AI Hate is Existential | Everybody's Business

Bloomberg Podcasts(467.0K) ·37:22 ·249 views · 2026-04-17
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
Public sentiment toward AI has shifted from optimism to existential dread, leading to physical violence against tech leaders and a "broken" job market where recent college graduates face significant underemployment and a mismatch between education and industry needs.
TL;DR Public sentiment toward AI has shifted from optimism to existential dread, leading to physical violence against tech leaders and a "broken" job market where recent college graduates face significant underemployment and a mismatch between education and industry needs....

TL;DR

Public sentiment toward AI has shifted from optimism to existential dread, leading to physical violence against tech leaders and a "broken" job market where recent college graduates face significant underemployment and a mismatch between education and industry needs.

The Rise of AI Backlash and Physical Threats

Recent events indicate a violent pivot in how the public perceives AI leaders and infrastructure.

  • Violent Incidents:
      • Sam Altman (OpenAI CEO): Targeted in an attempted arson involving a Molotov cocktail at his residence; the suspect, Daniel Moreno Gamma, faces attempted murder charges.
      • Indianapolis Data Center: A local councilman who approved a data center had bullets fired at his home with a note reading "no data centers."
  • Public Sentiment Shift: According to Pew Research, 52% of people are now more concerned than excited about AI, compared to only 10% who are more excited than concerned.
  • Top Public Concerns: Surprisingly, job loss is not the primary fear.
      • Erosion of Humanity: 27% cite the erosion of human abilities and connections.
      • Information Integrity: 18% worry about the negative impact on information accuracy.
      • Job Loss: Ranked only fifth on the list of primary concerns.
  • Marketing Backfire: Sarah Frier (Bloomberg Managing Editor) notes that CEOs like Sam Altman have marketed AI as "dangerous" and "nuclear" to justify slow rollouts, which inadvertently stoked existential fear in the public.

The "Broken" Youth Job Market

The promise of "learning to code" has collided with a surplus of graduates and a contraction in entry-level opportunities.

  • Unemployment Disparity: The unemployment rate for 18-24-year-olds is approximately 8%, double the general population.
  • The "Learn to Code" Failure:
      • Oversupply: Between 2014 and 2024, entry-level openings for computer science grew by 6%, but the number of graduates in that field grew by 110%.
      • Underemployment: Many degree-holders are taking roles that do not require their $200,000 education, a phenomenon that can depress salary growth for a decade.
  • AI Natives vs. AI Dependency: While the Class of 2026 is fluent in LLMs, Julia Rubin (Businessweek Senior Editor) observes they often lack subject matter expertise and "soft skills," such as presenting the slide decks the AI creates for them.

The Return of the "Human" Worker

As AI automates technical "grunt work," industries are re-evaluating the value of liberal arts and interpersonal skills.

  • The Management Consulting Crisis: Firms are concerned that AI-generated "slop" is becoming indistinguishable from junior associate output, leading to a renewed focus on human soft skills.
  • "Learn to Ode": Julia Rubin notes a shift where companies are once again looking for English majors to provide human intuition and communication skills that AI lacks.
  • Education System Failure: The current crisis is attributed to a "broken" education system where colleges over-enroll students in popular majors without regard for market capacity.

The "Mordor" Economy and Corporate Pivots

The high energy demands of AI are creating a feedback loop of rising costs and bizarre corporate rebranding.

  • The Mordor Economy: Stacy Vanek Smith references the "Mordor" concept (coined by Nate Hagens)—an economy where growth is swallowed by the massive energy needs of data centers and resource extraction.
      • Energy Poverty: Low-income households are now spending roughly 20% of their income on energy costs.
  • The Allbirds Pivot: In a stark example of "AI washing," the struggling sustainable shoe brand Allbirds recently spun off its footwear business to become Newbird AI.
      • Strategy: The company is pivoting toward "GPUaaS" (GPUs as a Service) and hardware.
      • Market Reaction: Despite the lack of an obvious connection between shoes and chips, the stock surged 373% following the announcement.

Notable Quotes

"The vast majority of people are not seeing yet the way that AI is enriching them. Instead, they’re seeing the way that it’s taking things away, whether that’s employment or resources, or just the threat that it might one day take something away." — Sarah Frier, Bloomberg Managing Editor
"They [companies] have marketed this technology as... 'really, this is so powerful, we need to roll this out slowly, take our time because if we don’t, the results could be disastrous.' It is the strangest public relations strategy of all time." — Sarah Frier, Bloomberg Managing Editor
"If we’re sort of automating away the grunt work or the sort of technical work, then perhaps the work that is more about being a human is going to be much more important." — Julia Rubin, Businessweek Senior Editor
"We are now living in the Mordor economy... an economy that's all about energy. Energy prices get more expensive, so the economy has to make more energy in order to feed the energy needs, and it becomes like a snake swallowing its own tail." — Stacy Vanek Smith, Bloomberg Host

Demis Hassabis Wants to Build AGI. Should We Trust Him?

The Next Big Idea Podcast(33.7K) ·69:18 ·183 views · 2026-04-17
Demis Hassabis (AI Chief, Google) Sebastian Mallaby (Journalist)
Demis Hassabis, the Nobel-winning founder of DeepMind, is driven by a unique blend of scientific rigor and "spiritual" ambition to build AGI, balancing a high-stakes rivalry with OpenAI against his desire to remain a "good" steward of humanity's most powerful invention.
TL;DR Demis Hassabis, the Nobel-winning founder of DeepMind, is driven by a unique blend of scientific rigor and "spiritual" ambition to build AGI, balancing a high-stakes rivalry with OpenAI against his desire to remain a "good" steward of humanity's most powerful invention. A...

TL;DR

Demis Hassabis, the Nobel-winning founder of DeepMind, is driven by a unique blend of scientific rigor and "spiritual" ambition to build AGI, balancing a high-stakes rivalry with OpenAI against his desire to remain a "good" steward of humanity's most powerful invention.

The Evolution of a "Jedi" Polymath

  • A Diverse Background: Hassabis’s journey to the top of Google’s AI efforts began as a child chess prodigy (ranking 2nd in the world for his age) and a video game designer of the hit *Theme Park* at age 17.
      • Parental Influence: His character is defined by a tension between his mother’s Christian values of compassion and his father’s "win-at-all-costs" competitive drive.
      • Academic Range: Unlike many Silicon Valley leaders, Hassabis is a deep scientist, holding a PhD in neuroscience which he used to model artificial intelligence after the human brain’s "action and perception" mechanisms.
  • Charisma and Control: Journalist Sebastian Mallaby describes Hassabis as having a "Jedi" quality—an ability to stare people in the eye and make them believe his vision for the future.
      • The "Good vs. Great" Conflict: While Hassabis strives to be a moral leader, Mallaby notes a "blind spot" regarding his controlling nature, evidenced by the 2019 departure of co-founder Mustafa Suleyman.

Scientific Breakthroughs: From Atari to the Nobel Prize

  • The Core Innovation: DeepMind’s early success was built on fusing two distinct AI traditions:
      • Deep Learning: Identifying patterns in massive datasets.
      • Reinforcement Learning: Learning through experience and feedback (trial and error).
  • AlphaGo to AlphaFold: After defeating the world champion in the game of Go, Hassabis immediately pivoted to biology, recognizing that the "combinatorial space" of games was the perfect training ground for scientific discovery.
      • AlphaFold Success: By solving the 50-year-old "protein folding" problem, DeepMind provided a tool to the global scientific community that is now accelerating drug discovery and medicine.
      • Algorithmic Invention: Mallaby argues this proves AI labs are doing deep "algorithmic invention," not just scaling up data centers.
  • Open Science: In a show of "virtue ethics," Hassabis made the AlphaFold database open-source and free for all scientists worldwide.

The OpenAI Rivalry and the LLM Blind Spot

  • Missing the Language Wave: Despite DeepMind being the "OG" of AI labs, they were caught off guard by ChatGPT.
      • The Gaia Project: Hassabis was focused on "Gaia," a simulation of the natural world, believing AGI required an agent to act in physical space rather than just processing "symbols" (language).
      • Shift in Strategy: The release of GPT-3 in 2020 forced Hassabis to pivot DeepMind's resources toward Large Language Models (LLMs) to catch up with OpenAI.
  • Perceptions of Rivals: According to Mallaby, Hassabis views his competitors through specific lenses:
      • Sam Altman: Driven primarily by the pursuit of power.
      • Mark Zuckerberg: Driven by shareholder value and product addiction.
      • Elon Musk: Seen as a "hypocritical" figure who oscillates between "doomsterism" and aggressive acceleration.

Existential Risk and the "Spiritual" Quest

  • AGI as Faith: Hassabis views the quest for superintelligence as a spiritual endeavor akin to "the God of Spinoza"—the idea that understanding nature through science brings one closer to the divine.
  • The Probability of Doom (P-Doom): While Hassabis remains "cautiously optimistic," the briefing highlights a growing concern regarding AI safety.
      • The Survival Instinct: Geoffrey Hinton argues that we may accidentally grant AI a survival instinct by tasking it with defending itself against cyberattacks.
      • The "Race Condition": There is a persistent tension between Hassabis's safety principles and the market pressure to release "open-weight" models (like Google’s Gemma) to compete with rivals.
  • Conclusion on Character: Mallaby concludes that Hassabis is "both great and good," but acknowledges that the "greatness" of the task often requires a pragmatism that can conflict with pure moral idealism.

Notable Quotes

"If you're right... it follows that you as the creator of AI will be one of the most important people in the history of humanity, which means you don't have a choice. Somebody's going to write a book about you." — Sebastian Mallaby (recalling his pitch to Hassabis)
"He [Hassabis] is the only person who was really at the origin of the science and also a lab leader... Sam Altman, not a scientist, dropped out of Stanford, very different kettle of fish." — Sebastian Mallaby
"We are defined by the desire to invent as human beings and he [Hassabis] is part of that and he is leading us on a frightening ride, but it's kind of human destiny to go there." — Sebastian Mallaby
"You put those three things together: superintelligence, survival instinct, and devious obfuscation. It feels like analytically I can't be confident that we're not going to be attacked." — Sebastian Mallaby (on the risks of AGI)
I'm Caleb Bissinger. And I'm Rufus Griscom. And this is the Next Big Idea. Today, Demis Hassabis wants to build superintelligence. Should we trust him? For three years, two men met regularly at a pub in a leafy neighborhood in North London. They'd climb the rickety wooden stairs to the second floor, and there, in a room that was always empty, they'd sink into leather chairs beneath a tarnished chandelier and talk for hours. One of the men was journalist Sebastian Mallaby. The other was a local boy, a chess prodigy who'd grown up to be a video game designer, a neuroscientist, a Nobel laureate in chemistry, and one of the most important and powerful figures in the world of artificial intelligence. His name is Demis Hassabis. Those conversations, along with interviews with more than 100 people in Demis's orbit, are the grist of Sebastian's new book, *The Infinity Machine*. It does all the standard biography stuff, charting Demis's improbable journey to becoming the guy in charge of Google's AI. But what really got me interested in Sebastian's book is something he writes in the introduction. A new kind of intelligence, he says, is being willed into the world by a remarkably small number of people. Each of them is driven by a particular mix of curiosity and hubris, vanity and avarice, idealism and craving. And the sobering reality is that for better or worse, the quality of their characters will affect society. So, what motivates Demis? What are his talents, his flaws? What does he think of his rivals? What does he think the world will look like if he succeeds? And if he really believes there's a chance AI could destroy us, why keep building it? These questions affect all of us, and we'll try to answer them right after the break. And then Rufus, why don't you come back at the end of the show and you and I can talk about what this all means. Yeah, let's do that. I mean, I can't get over this notion that there are half a dozen people leading the top AI labs in the world, and how they think and what drives them arguably could not be more important. Sebastian, welcome to the Next Big Idea. So great to be with you. I have this post-it note stuck to my notes for this interview here, and it's a quote from Henry Luce, the founder of *Time* Magazine. He believed that what journalists should do—and this is what I've written on the post-it note—is tell the history of our time through the people who make it. And I think that's what you've done with your new book, *The Infinity Machine*, about Demis Hassabis. And I think that's what we're going to do over the next hour on the show: try to tell the history of our time through the people who make it. And so I want to begin by asking how you chose your particular history maker, how you chose Demis. He is not as kind of messianic as Elon Musk, he's not as Machiavellian as Sam Altman, he's not as pugnacious as Dario Amodei, he's not as Pollyannaish as Reid Hoffman. So what is it that drew you to him? Why write a book about him? Partly it was that he was the OG, the first guy to get into this game. You know, he set up DeepMind in 2010, four or five years before OpenAI was founded by Elon and Sam. And even before 2010, he had been dreaming about building AI since he went to college in the mid-1990s at Cambridge University in England. So that was one thing. Through his life story, you can tell the making of modern AI because he does stretch back to the origins. And then he led all of the stuff that went on really until ChatGPT came out. You know, his lab, DeepMind in London, was clearly the top AI lab in the world. And then there was this exciting fight in the last part of the story which made it even better because, you know, ChatGPT came out and Google and DeepMind—Demis Hassabis's team—were kind of behind. And so there's an exciting catch-up story at the end of the book. We'll get into that. And part of what made it, I think, particularly painful for Demis and the team at Google was that a lot of what allowed ChatGPT to work was building on research and breakthroughs that Google had made themselves, that DeepMind had made themselves. And so what is it about Demis that led him into this project and what do you think it is about you that made him feel comfortable opening up? Well, you know, I went and pitched him. I was very lucky. I went and pitched him in November 2022 on the idea of spending super long amounts of time with me so I could write a book about him. And I thought that he was fascinating. I'd known him for a few years at conferences and stuff. I also thought AI was fascinating, would maybe be a bit niche, but was going to break out at some point. And then a week after I pitched Demis, ChatGPT dropped and it went from the fringe to the mainstream way faster than I expected. But what I said to him in the pitch was really two things. First is, all of his lectures had been saying for a long time that artificial intelligence would be the most important invention in the history of humanity. So I said to him quite simply, look, if you're right, it follows that you as the creator of AI will be one of the most important people in the history of humanity. Which means you don't have a choice. Somebody's going to write a book about you. You may be able to decide which particular writer you spend time with, but you can't negate the reality that there will be books, plural, about you. So let's just get real about that. Second thing I said is, look, if you are going to disrupt human life on the scale that you say—with, you know, people bringing up their kids in a new way because why would they learn to write if chatbots can do it for them, people thinking of themselves as humans in a different way because we used to define ourselves as the most intelligent species in the world and now there's this machine intelligence that might surpass us... If you're going to disrupt people from head to toe, you owe them an explanation of why you're doing it. What motivates you? Why do something this dangerous? And unless you provide that explanation, nobody in society's going to accept it. And, you know, I presented myself as somebody who had written other books. I try to understand the people I write about. It's independent, but I'm not out to, you know, knuckle people gratuitously because I've been around the block. I'm 61, I've done five other books. I know that all humans are flawed, including me. Like the way you set it up in the early pages, you almost frame it around a question of virtue ethics. Demis does seem to be a good person who has good principles, who is pursuing this for good reasons. And yet his character, his temperament, his integrity, his choice of where to stand in moments of challenge—those things are going to matter not just for him and for his company, but for humanity. And that there's this tension constantly in him between his desire for discovery and his desire for scientific breakthrough. Speak to that. Anything you want to add there? Yeah, I mean, you're right. That question of whether you can be both great and good is kind of at the core of the book. I think in history, if you look back at people who are termed "great," whether it's Alexander the Great or Catherine the Great or Peter the Great, you know, these are all people who basically led armies and killed a lot of people. I think we sometimes feel like, because ChatGPT exploded and now AI is everywhere, we sort of forget that people were working on AI for a really long time before it hit the mainstream. Around 1993 or so, he was admitted to Cambridge University, but he was very young—he was only 16 or so. So I think a lot of people who meet Demis Hassabis think this is the guy. He just has that magic. Did you think that when you met him? I did. I thought he was remarkable. I didn't realize the full depth of how interesting he is, how he can switch from discussion of philosophy to movies to theoretical physics to neuroscience, you know, and do it all seeming totally intelligible, approachable, non-arrogant, friendly. You know, his colleagues in his first startup used to joke that he was the Jedi because he would stare you in the eye and say, "You will believe these things," and then people did believe them. So he has a kind of magic. But in addition to that, more seriously and scientifically, what DeepMind did very early was to fuse the two central traditions in artificial intelligence research. The first one is deep learning, which is essentially learning from enormous quantities of data and trying to find patterns in that data. And that's what underpins the early ChatGPT versions, you know, much later on. But the second kind of artificial intelligence is called reinforcement learning, and that is learning from experience. The system tries an action, it gets a feedback signal from its environment. So it might be playing a game, for example, and it plays a game, plays move, opponent plays a move and at the end it either wins or loses and that's a feedback signal which helps it to understand if its moves that it tried were good. And by repeating millions and millions of games, it gets a lot of feedback signals, and then that is how it learns stuff from trial and error and feedback. So these were two separate traditions historically in AI; they were embodied by Geoffrey Hinton at Toronto and then Richard Sutton, the sort of father of reinforcement learning who was based in Edmonton in Canada. So two British scientists, both of them operating out of Canadian colleges. And there was a scientist called Vlad Mnih of Ukrainian extraction, a Canadian-Ukrainian, and he had done graduate work with both of these two traditions, and DeepMind hired him. And because of Vlad and because of others also who were minded to try and bring together the two traditions, that's what they pulled off with the Atari game-playing system which they did in 2012. That was a breakthrough. I mean, it was kind of ImageNet, which was, you know, the ability to recognize photographs. ImageNet was pure deep learning, and what DeepMind contributed was an ImageNet equivalent but for agentic systems which were using reinforcement learning in addition to deep learning. I should say as an aside there's this really funny moment in the book where Demis is starting DeepMind and he's trying to raise money and one of his investors says you have to come to my house and you have to pitch me and my wife and my 14-year-old son and we all get a vote and you have to convince three of us if I'm going to write a check into your business. And Demis is like, oh my God I have to explain this to a 14-year-old kid. I am that 14-year-old kid. So when you're talking about the Atari system, it's basically like they're using deep learning in order to allow this AI to see and understand the gameplay and then they're using reinforcement learning for it actually to play and through trial and error figure out what are good moves and what are bad moves? Is that how those two overlap? Yeah, I mean that's the start of the explanation. I mean Demis had, you know, done his PhD interestingly not in computer science but in neuroscience. And his reason for that was he wanted to understand how human intelligence worked because that was the existence proof for artificial intelligence being possible. One of the big ideas he got from doing neuroscience is that intelligence consists of many different components inside the brain, right? So you have the hippocampus and the cerebellum and so forth and so forth. And in layman's terms, there are different bits of the brain which some of which do planning, some of which do memory, some of which do imagination and so forth. And what's really magical is when you put them all together and they interact really cleverly, that produces real intelligence. And so with Atari they were applying that intuition. And so for example, one of the things that they did was that the system would play many, many games to try to get feedback signals about whether moves were good or bad. So you've got all these moves and you've got all these feedback signals, and then you want to learn from those pairs of action-reward what moves will lead you to victory in the future. And for complicated reasons I won't get into, the system won't learn well if it tries to derive conclusions in real-time with each feedback signal. Because it will over-index on sort of one micro part of the game where your paddle is stuck in the top right-hand corner, it's doing these repetitive shots in the top right and that will kind of mislead it. What you do is you collect like a million different pairs of action-reward, you put them in a memory bank, and then you have a bunch of data in the memory bank and then you can do deep learning as if it was ImageNet recognizing a photograph on top of all those stored experiences. It's quite a subtle series of ways of combining the idea of reinforcement learning to collect win-loss feedback and then deep learning on top of a memory bank of those results to produce a game system that wins. You know just a minute ago you were saying it's an almost dizzying experience spending time with Demis because he'll be talking about a movie and then he's talking about theoretical physics and then he's talking about biology, like he just sees this all as part of this stew that he lives and thinks in. I'm thinking about that because you're also now saying that you have these oil and water camps, right: deep learning, reinforcement learning. They're completely separate tribes. And Demis says these can be united, these can overlap and these can intermingle, much as he does in conversation, you know, connecting threads from all... Do you think there's—does that say something about the way he thinks, the way he sees the world? 100%. I think he's Mr. Anti-Silo, right? He's super cross-disciplinary. And actually over the holidays at the end of last year I was having a break between finishing this book and then needing to come on podcasts and talk about it. And I read a book called *Range* by David Epstein. And, you know, it makes the point that a lot of the people who do the best, whether it's in sports or in music or in lots of fields, they have range. They don't just specialize in one thing and do whatever it is, you know, 3,000 hours of practice and then they're great. That's the kind of Malcolm Gladwell learning and maybe I got the number wrong, but the point is dogged specialization wins. No, no, no it doesn't, actually. Experimentation, allowing you to bring inspiration from one subject into another one, tends to do better if you're really going for excellence. And Demis embodies that.
