So if we're thinking about the breakthroughs and the innovations that have made DeepMind sort of the cornerstone of modern AI, one is uniting deep learning and reinforcement learning, as you just described. Another is embracing the transformer, embracing transformer architecture, which is something that emerges out of Google I think in 2017 and DeepMind embraces. Describe what that technology is, why it's so significant to today's AI. Sure. So with ImageNet, which was sort of the big breakthrough for images, what the system was really doing was understanding that each pixel in a photograph is probably going to be related to the pixel right next to it. Because, you know, they're both part of the cat's tail or something. And that intuition that there is a relationship between different bits of the puzzle you're trying to solve is helpful in narrowing down the search space that you have to conquer when you're figuring out what the picture is. Now, in sequences, sequential data—think about, you know, a text, lots of words. Different words in the sentence will relate to each other in different ways. It's not the same as a picture where you kind of have clusters of related pixels. Because also in a sentence you might have a reference back to something that happened in the previous sentence. If I say, "I dropped my keys," and then the second sentence is, "I can't get into my house without them." The 'them' is the pronoun that makes no sense unless you can relate it back to the first sentence. And to unravel the meaning of sequential data—and that could also be not just text, it could be the clips in a movie, it could be the ticker numbers in a stock market... Any sequential data, you need to figure out the relationship between the different bits in the sequence. And to make a long story short, the point about the transformer innovation was that it was the first time you could really solve the meaning of big amounts of sequential data. You could put a whole book into a transformer and it would understand all of the interrelationships in that sequence. You could put, you know, a whole movie in, etc. And so really that was the significance of the 2017 paper. And it brings speed to the process, right? Because it doesn't have to understand things token by token, word by word, because that would be an incredibly slow and time-consuming and laborious process for an AI to go about understanding a book. If it's taking it token by token, the transformer allows it to sort of see it almost in multi-dimensional space and therefore understand it and engage with it much, much, much faster. Correct. And you know, the funny thing was that the GPU chips that were being used by this point, you know, ubiquitously for AI, had this feature that they could parallelize operations. And yet that ability to parallelize things hadn't been fully exploited until the transformer model came along. And then the transformer architecture, you know, ironically tackled sequences without paying attention to the order in which these sequences played out. They were able to feed enormous amounts of text; kind of different bits of that text simultaneously would be processed and you didn't have to do it sequentially. That liberation, as you're suggesting, was why you could suddenly deal with way more data much, much faster because you weren't doing it ploddingly, you know, one token after the other. How did all this coalesce to allow Demis and DeepMind to solve the problem of protein folding? Well, that was very interesting because the original impetus for taking on protein folding, aside from the fact that Demis had been personally fascinated by it since he was at college... Because a friend of his had mentioned the protein folding problem back when he was at college... like while they were playing like table football, right? Like one night as a stray comment at a bar. Yeah, that's right. And that's how—kind of how Demis operates, right: he's a magpie, he collects these bits of interesting challenge or information, puts them in the back of his mind and then bang, something happens later. Could be a long time later—and he thinks, "Oh, I remember, yeah this is now the right time to take on that thing I heard about in the college bar when I was 19." And so the moment this 'aha' moment came that this was the right time was when DeepMind had defeated the South Korean Go champion in a kind of exhibition match in Seoul. The system called AlphaGo had defeated the human and Demis was walking out of the exhibition hall where the game took place with his friend David Silver. Coming down the street in Seoul—and we know this because coincidentally there was a camera with a microphone kind of following them and this was picked up—Demis says, "We've done that, we've done that, we've solved Go, so the next thing is we can solve protein folding." And what he meant was that Go had this immense combinatorial space, right? You have 19 by 19 on the board, that means there's 361 squares. That means the first move there's 361 possibilities and then the second there's 360 and so forth. And very soon you multiply all these options together: move, counter move, move, counter move, and you get to this impossibly large number of combinations. To solve that game had been a grand challenge in computer science precisely because of the huge combinatorial complexity. And the point about protein folding was that with protein folding you have this strip of amino acid and on it there's the DNA code. And the DNA code governs how it's going to do a self-executing origami shape. The strand of amino acid folds itself up into a super complex, intricate shape. And then these are the building blocks of nature. And to predict the shape just from the amino acids means predicting a shape that could be one of billions, trillions, you know, a massive number of possible shapes because each little atom can twist either way. And so it's—it's just enormously complicated. But Demis's point was "Hey, if we solved Go, which was a big combinatorial space, the next thing is obviously AlphaFold." And there you see precisely how you can't pigeonhole him, you know: it goes from games to structural biology in the same breath. So he sets DeepMind to work on this. And the first idea is, you know, David Silver having conquered Go with reinforcement learning, will apply reinforcement learning to AlphaFold, to the idea of protein folding. And they would have the same recipe. And after not very long, like six months or so, they quickly understand that that's not going to work. And they basically go through a series of experiments where they pivot and they pivot, and another thing doesn't work and they pivot again. And along the way, there emerges this young scientist called John Jumper, who had been obsessed with protein folding forever. He brought in the transformer model. I guess this was sort of 2018-2019 when he brought it in, and so it had been invented a year or a year and a half before. And he decided that the sequences of the DNA on the amino acid strands which make up protein could be subjected to a transformer-style sequential modeling tool. He used the transformer and he threw out the reinforcement learning. There was no reinforcement learning in AlphaFold. And so, you know, when I think it's interesting about this story and all the pivots that went into it and finally, you know, solving all of the shapes in nature and getting a Nobel Prize for it, is that this achievement completely belies the nonsense that some people say where they say, "Well, the labs aren't doing algorithmic invention, they are merely scaling." "They're just obsessed with building bigger and bigger data centers, there's no real invention," you know. Nonsense. Nonsense. You know, this was exactly the opposite. There was enormous algorithmic experimentation, pivoting and innovation. This is also an area where what will flow from it will be profound for human beings, right? It's not just creating funny videos; it's going to unlock drug discovery, advance medicine... So the consequences for humanity of figuring out protein folding are remarkable and not to be underestimated. You've alluded to this already in the conversation but there's this sort of, you know, DeepMind's kind of riding high, they've figured out protein folding, and yet it's OpenAI, a lab started by Sam Altman and Elon Musk, in part because they didn't trust Google and Demis to be the only people building and potentially controlling AGI. And it's OpenAI using some of the breakthroughs that DeepMind had made to create and launch ChatGPT, something that, you know, DeepMind was not even really particularly focused on building a large language model, an LLM, at the time that ChatGPT launched. What was going on? Was Demis just asleep at the wheel? Like how did they—I mean, in retrospect it looks like a pretty big blind spot. Right, right. And I would ask him this, you know, repeatedly when we were up there on the top floor of that pub, in the kind of secret room that nobody knew about and there was just the two of us and we had two hours together each month. And the most interesting part of the answer as to why he missed this and let OpenAI get ahead went back to his studies of neuroscience. Because in neuroscience there is an idea called action and perception, which basically says that intelligence requires the intelligent agent to act in the world. You won't know what gravity is until you've felt it, until you've dropped an object and seen it fall to the floor. You wouldn't know the difference between liquid and solid unless you had a glass of water and you tipped it and then the liquid fell out and splashed. You need to understand the physical world, and that's how humans do a lot of their learning and therefore by extension artificial intelligence will either need to be robotic and act in the world or at the minimum act in game simulations of the real world. And so Demis was pursuing that vision of how you get to super intelligence, and he had a fascinating project called Gaia. The idea was to build a simulation of the natural environment, which is way more irregular and complex to act in than a man-made environment. You know, there's no right angles in nature, you get these enormous trees and tiny little blades of grass; it's super irregular and therefore very hard to impose categories on it. And that's something which is part of what intelligent beings do: they kind of classify objects to understand the world. So the idea was to build Gaia and get to super intelligence that way. And then another project he had going on was an agent that played the real-time multiplayer game StarCraft 2. And the point again was that this is a super complicated game which requires you to plan over long horizons, you can't see the whole landscape, you have to therefore act in a fog of uncertainty—can an AI agent handle that? was the question. And in the meantime what he was not doing so much was focusing on language. And his view was, "Look, language is a system of symbols, and you can understand symbols—great, but we know from action and perception and neuroscience that that ain't enough, that won't get you to super intelligence." And he basically persisted in this conviction until 2020, when GPT-3 came out. And that was really quite a strong model. And suddenly Demis said, "Wow, okay, I was wrong. We need to catch up. We're going to do language big time." And he threw a ton of resources and started to try to catch up. In that effort to catch up, it does seem like Demis had to betray some of his principles about how AI should scale. Right, he's always been aware that if things go right with this technology it will be wonderful for humanity, but there are very real risks that it goes wrong and if it does it will be catastrophic. And so he's always been concerned about safety, speed, and avoiding a "race condition" where you have multiple labs racing each other and taking big risks. And all of a sudden he finds himself thrust into this world where, oh my gosh, we're on our back foot, we gotta catch ChatGPT, and now they have another new model coming out and it's better than ours, and oh my gosh Anthropic just released this model and it's incredible, and oh my gosh the Chinese just released this lightweight model and it's maybe better than... Like all of a sudden he has to—you know, we've been talking since the beginning—can you be good and can you also be great? That tension is right there, right? He has to decide: do I get into this race because that's what the company wants and that's what the company needs, but does that mean sacrificing some of my principles around safety and speed? Yeah. And we saw an example of that actually just last week, when DeepMind released a new suite of its open-weight light models called Gemma. And the reason that's revealing is that, you know, Demis would say, has said to me, look, open-source models can be very dangerous because people can download them, modify them, do whatever they want with them and you can't control that. Whereas if you serve them a proprietary model, A, they can't mess around with the safeguards you've put on it, and B, if they start to use it for a cyber attack or something you can switch it off. And so you give up those things with open-weight, and yet he's going ahead with open-weight. So that really illustrates how he's doing stuff that he himself would recognize to be sub-optimal for safety. And, you know, the big I think interesting, exciting, terrifying thing to be watching in this AI race right now is how all of the different lab leaders process this position that they are in because they're all in it. And we saw Dario Amodei from Anthropic pick a fight with the Pentagon and try and assert some principles about how AI is used for mass surveillance or for autonomous lethal weapons. Basically he got rolled, indeed attacked by the Pentagon, and he's having to fight law cases to protect the understanding of whether American labs can work with him or not. So, you know, you could say he made a principled stand, but you could also say not clear it changed the world for the better and it might have damaged his company, although I think hopefully he'll be fine. I think, you know, Sam Altman on this very day that we're recording, Monday, has just put out a big essay about how to manage the kind of labor market disruption and inequality increases that will come from AI. So he's making a public stand about it. Demis believes, I think, at least as strongly as either of them, and probably a lot—a lot more strongly than Sam does—that he wants to be good and that he would like AI to be beneficial for society. He built AlphaFold and, by the way, gave it to the world. It's totally open-source; any scientist in the world can use it for free. He does this because that is AI for the good. It's going to advance medicine for the good, you know, and he's very proud of that and rightly so. I don't know if you, Sebastian, watch a lot of reality TV. Something tells me you probably don't. I don't. There's something they do on these shows called a confessional, where they take one cast member and they put them in a room with the cameras and they ask them to sort of say what they really think about the other cast members on the show. If we were to do that with Demis and we said, "What do you really think about your rivals Dario, Sam, Elon? What do you think he would say?" Well, I've done that with him. And, you know, what he says is that Sam wants power. Sam thought of running for Governor of California; there's rumors that he even wanted to run for President. You know, his objective function is power, and that's how he sees Sam. And that's not something he says with respect. He doesn't like people who are trying to maximize power. I think about Mark Zuckerberg he would say that guy wants money. He wants to maximize the shareholder value. He wants Meta to roll out even more addictive products with the help of AI. I think, you know, Elon is an interesting one because Elon's record on this is so double standards and hypocritical, right? He was very, very outspoken at various times about the existential risk posed by AI. And yet he's also very, very determined to race and have xAI be a leading player in the field. He's both; he's an accelerationist and a doomster at once. I think for whatever reason Demis doesn't like to criticize Elon, partly because maybe Elon has such a megaphone that poking the bear is unwise. I'm speculating there, but you know there have been times when Elon would go around saying, "Well, Demis Hassabis is an evil genius and he's going to destroy us all and that's why we need to have OpenAI as a counterweight to DeepMind." Demis doesn't want to reignite that kind of talk. So, you know, he's a bit tactful about—about Elon. One of the things that's interesting to me with Demis is the way that he talks about this technology. It is different than those guys, different from Sam or Elon, which is that it's infused with a kind of religiosity. It's infused with a kind of faith, and he says to you that his pursuit of AGI is a spiritual endeavor, that it's about trying to get close to God, I think is the word he uses. Does that draw from—from the faith that he grew up with with his mother? Is that where does that come from? To me that's highly unusual, this sense that he's driven not by power, not by wealth, but by faith. Yeah, I mean it's true he did grow up with a religious mother. He did pray in the evenings as a child, he did sing in the choir at the church, and he has that tradition which probably makes it more natural for him to reach for religious terms, spiritual terms to describe what he feels about his scientific quest to understand nature. I mean, we talked about this at some length and sometimes he would try to discourage me from seeing him as spiritual, although he confessed that he did feel it was spiritual. But he didn't want me to overdo that because what he—what he was saying was look, you know, I—I'm a scientist primarily. I want to understand nature. And I choose to sometimes describe that incredibly intense urge to understand nature as being a kind of quest for quasi-spiritual understanding. And I'm not the only one to do this. Demis would carry on, he said you know Einstein talked about the God of Spinoza. Spinoza being the 17th-century European philosopher who—who said that, you know, nature is God's creation and to understand nature as a scientist will get you closer to understanding God. And Demis feels that. And I have scientific friends who have nothing to do with this book, but who when I describe this to them say, "Oh yeah, I feel the same." So I think it's not that unusual for scientists, particularly who are doing things like theoretical physics, to be so passionate as to be spiritual about it. And I think the last thing to say about this is that what we're skirting around here is the topic of how do human beings process the enormity of AGI, of—of being the one to discover AGI? And, you know, some of the time what I observed was people had weird reactions. So Shane Legg, the co-founder of DeepMind, gave a famous lecture which was where he actually bonded with Demis Hassabis for the first time, and this was in 2009 and it was about AI risk. Back in 2009 it was about the possibility that AI would become sufficiently powerful to threaten human existence. And as Shane is delivering this lecture, and I could watch it on a—on a video which exists on the internet, you know you could see him smiling and his eyes are kind of blowing and he kind of giggles. And as he describes more intense existential risk, that giggle gets more prominent. And you think, what is going on? Why is it funny to talk about potential human annihilation? And the fact is that when we humans contemplate our own annihilation, it feels absurd, and the absurd is a close cousin of humor, and so you laugh. That came up a bit in my research. And then another way of processing the enormity of what's going on is to sort of reach for religious symbols and language. And so there's this moment when Ilya Sutskever is talking to his fellow scientists at OpenAI at a company retreat. There's a fire pit and it's the evening, and Ilya produces a kind of effigy and puts it in the fire and said, "This stands for the evil AGI, and when the evil AGI comes we have to burn it." And they burn the effigy. And it was sort of super kind of culty religious feeling thing, yeah you know. And so I think the symbolism of religion is somehow what we turn to to describe the ineffable nature of a super intelligence that's super hard to get your head around otherwise. Despite the ineffable nature of—of AGI, Demis remains cautiously optimistic. That's the phrase he used with you. Why do you think that is? Like how do you think he's managed to justify that position given—given what he knows about the potential risks and dangers here? You know, I think some of it is that you have to say you're optimistic if you're going to carry on. You have to justify yourself, come up with a rationale for—for what you're doing. But I think also there is a Rorschach test which goes on with the prospect of AGI when people compare their so-called probability of doom, the P. How likely is it that humans would be wiped out or partially wiped out by—by AGI? And I think where people land on that spectrum of P depends on their temperament. And, you know, someone like Geoffrey Hinton, the father of deep learning, is very, very doomsterish. But I know former PhD students of his who say that he was doomsterish about the prospect of bioterrorism before AI became powerful enough to worry about. He always had something that he was terrified of. That just rings true to me. When I get up every morning, you know, although analytically I see the case for being very worried about the robots attacking us, and we can go into why I think that, but I really do think there's a non-zero risk that this could happen... I can't feel upset on a daily basis. I just get up, I like the world, I like my job, I like my friends, I have a great time. I feel optimistic and so I'm optimistic still. To parrot Demis. And—and maybe he's just he's got that optimism which is kind of a requirement for an entrepreneur and when you have that, of course you're optimistic still. You can't tease us with that. What is your P, how worried are you about this circumstance where the robots rise up against us? And then maybe also on the flip side, I mean Demis is quite convincing in this book about what the beautiful, abundant, post-AGI world could look like if we get it right. So maybe play those two against each other. Sure. I mean I think the upside abundance I broadly agree with at least in—in the longer term. So, you know, I think AGI or even just powerful AI is going to accelerate scientific discovery. That's going to be terrific for medicine. It's going to unlock a lot of new learning and information for lots of people. It's going to maybe help material science which could in turn lead to inventions that reduce climate change. I mean, for example, nuclear fusion as a source of fuel has made strides in recent years, not least because of AI and the way that both AI can help you to invent new materials that are good for fusion reactions and also can help you to suspend the nuclear plasma in the reactor magnetically. You need a—a very sophisticated magnetic control function and reinforcement learning turns out to be a way of creating one. So I think the sort of abundance story is certainly true for scientific progress. And I think largely probably true for just general human lives. Because I think that we will as humans invent new things to be passionate about, to give our lives meaning, we will compete at games, we will do art, we will—we will occupy ourselves productively and stimulate ourselves. And so I don't buy the story that, you know, when we have no jobs we decline into depression. I think we'll figure that out because of just our—the way we are wired. Now on the downside, I spent a lot of time during my project. I spent, you know, more than three and a half years doing this. And for most of that time I was wandering around saying yeah, sure, the machines will be more intelligent than humans but they're not wired to compete with us. I mean, humans evolved over millennia to survive, to fear risks, and to fight to survive to pass on our DNA. And that was our most basic objective function. And machines don't have that. They're not evolved and they don't necessarily want to survive, and so why would they pick a fight with us? And in fact, Demis had told me about this series of books he read, that science fiction books called the Culture series by Iain Banks, where in an AGI future you have all these AGIs that are, you know, doing their own stuff. They—they have their own political intrigues, they play their own chess matches, they do their own space exploration and the humans live alongside them without being bothered by them and it's all perfectly amicable. I kind of thought, well, you know, I don't—I don't want to anchor my view on science fiction but I do see that. And then I went to Toronto one day and I sat in Geoffrey Hinton's kitchen for two hours and we debated why Geoffrey Hinton, having invented deep learning academically, is so pessimistic about what that's going to do to us today. And I came armed with this knowledge that, you know, on the Rorschach test I mentioned earlier he had always been super pessimistic. So, you know, maybe he was just overdoing it. And we had this debate where he basically explained to me why AI is going to want to survive and therefore I shouldn't be so optimistic. And what he said was look, supposing you create a very powerful AI and you're worried that it's going to be subject to a cyber attack by an enemy AI. You need to defend your own AI, but a human brain is not fast enough to protect against an AI-generated cyber attack. So you're going to have to empower your own AI to defend itself against the attacking AI. And you're going to have to tell your own AI, you need to survive here. Bang, you just did it. You gave it a survival instinct. And then there are all these experiments, by the way, that come out of the red teaming of models before they're released. And, you know, I think both Anthropic and OpenAI have released descriptions of some of these cases where not only does the AI want to survive, not only is it more intelligent than us in many ways, but it also lies to us. It's very capable of obfuscation and dissembling. You put those three things together: superintelligence, survival instinct, and devious obfuscation. It feels like analytically I can't be confident that we're not going to be attacked. That's certainly a grim place, an important message but a grim message to land on. Why don't we close with this: after three-plus years of working on this book, are you any closer to an answer on the question of whether it is possible to be both good and great? I'd say the answer depends on how you judge people. If you want them to be utterly pure and constant in their principles and never controlling of any one, then it's impossible to be both great and good. If you're willing to say, well in the real world people need to adjust their principles, they need to be pragmatic about when you take a principled stand—for example on safety and how you do it—don't use up your capital by taking a stand that wouldn't actually change outcomes in the real world, which I personally find compelling. Because I think judging somebody in the abstract, sort of separated from how the real world functions, is just not useful. I mean, we surely ought to be judged as human beings in the context of the world in which we live. And if you do that, then I think you can be sort of good on a relative basis, which ends up just being good. And so I'm willing to say that Demis Hassabis is both great and good. The book is The Infinity Machine. Sebastian, it's been really wonderful talking with you today. Thank you so much. I've enjoyed it so much. Great, thank you. Thank you.

Why the A.I. Backlash Turned Violent in America

Hard Fork(56.9K) ·60:31 ·2.5K views · 2026-04-17
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
Public sentiment toward AI is shifting toward radicalization and violence, driven by deep economic anxiety, a perceived lack of democratic oversight, and rising local opposition to massive data center infrastructure.
TL;DR Public sentiment toward AI is shifting toward radicalization and violence, driven by deep economic anxiety, a perceived lack of democratic oversight, and rising local opposition to massive data center infrastructure. Incidents of Physical Violence: Recent events suggest a...

TL;DR

Public sentiment toward AI is shifting toward radicalization and violence, driven by deep economic anxiety, a perceived lack of democratic oversight, and rising local opposition to massive data center infrastructure.

Radicalization and Violent Backlash

  • Incidents of Physical Violence: Recent events suggest a dangerous escalation in anti-AI sentiment from online discourse to physical attacks.
      • Sam Altman Attack: A 20-year-old suspect allegedly targeted the OpenAI CEO’s San Francisco home with a Molotov cocktail; the suspect possessed a manifesto opposing AI and a "hit list" of other tech executives and investors.
      • Indianapolis Shooting: More than a dozen gunshots were fired at the home of Councilman Ron Gibson following his vote to approve a new data center. A note left at the scene read "no data centers."
  • Trust Deficit: According to a Stanford AI Index report, public trust in the technology is plummeting.
      • Only 31% of Americans trust their government to regulate AI responsibly, compared to a global average of 54%.
  • The Rhetoric Bind: Host Casey Newton argues that AI CEOs have "inflamed" the situation by claiming their products pose an "existential threat" to humanity while simultaneously lobbying against safety regulations (such as California’s transparency bills).

The "Data Center NIMBY" Movement

  • Legislative Moratoriums: Local and federal politicians are increasingly moving to block the physical infrastructure required for AI.
      • Maine: Passed a temporary moratorium on data centers larger than 20 megawatts until 2027.
      • Wisconsin: Voters in Port Washington recently restricted the building of future data centers.
      • Federal Level: Bernie Sanders and Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez have proposed a national moratorium on data center construction.
  • Economic vs. Environmental Fears: While some view AI as an economic engine, local opposition is focusing on rising home energy costs, environmental degradation, and noise pollution.
  • Lever for Change: Host Kevin Roose notes that blocking data centers is the only "lever" average citizens feel they have left to slow a top-down technological revolution they did not ask for.

Silicon Valley’s Obsession with Longevity

  • Kara Swisher’s Critique: In an interview regarding her new documentary, *Kara Swisher Wants to Live Forever*, the veteran journalist characterizes the tech elite’s quest for immortality as a "narcissistic" distraction from systemic issues.
      • "The Wealth Gap": Swisher argues that the most effective way to increase longevity is universal healthcare, yet many tech leaders support political movements that reduce public health funding.
      • Stunts vs. Science: Swisher dismisses hyperbaric chambers and "biohacking" as "ridiculous" stunts for the rich, while identifying mRNA technology and CRISPR gene editing as the truly promising frontiers of medicine.
  • Healthspan vs. Lifespan: A central theme of the longevity movement is "collapsing" the period of decline at the end of life so that individuals remain healthy until shortly before death.

The Automation of the CEO

  • The "Zuck-Bot": Meta is reportedly building an AI version of Mark Zuckerberg to interact with staff.
      • Training Data: The bot is being trained on Zuckerberg’s specific mannerisms, tone, and strategic thinking to help employees feel "connected" to the founder.
      • C-Suite Utility: Hosts suggest that AI clones could eventually handle repetitive CEO tasks, such as testifying before foreign parliaments, conducting "pre-calls" for events, or answering routine internal inquiries.
  • Synthetic Media Shift: The project mirrors a broader shift within Meta platforms (Instagram, Facebook) away from human connection and toward synthetic, AI-generated content.

Notable Quotes

"Words have power too... someone said to me yesterday they thought [an article] was coming at a time of great anxiety about AI and that it made things more dangerous for me." — Sam Altman (via blog post)
"No one is going to stop the march of AI with a few stray bullets... but it is the CEOs themselves who have been inflaming the rhetoric... telling us that superintelligence is imminent." — Casey Newton
"Death acceptance makes you live longer. Death denial makes you hateful, tiny, and dying quicker. Scientific." — Kara Swisher
"I think the fear and the expectation among the leaders of these companies is that it will get much worse if and when AI does actually start to cause mass disruption to the labor market." — Kevin Roose
I’m Kevin Roose, a tech columnist at The New York Times. I’m Casey Newton from Platformer. And this is Hard Fork. This week, the AI backlash has turned violent. We’ll debate what’s making it so unpopular. Then, Kara Swisher returns to the show to discuss her new documentary on Silicon Valley’s obsession with living longer. And finally, can CEOs replace themselves with AI? Mark Zuckerberg may be giving it a shot. And we wish him the best. All right, Kevin. Well, some very serious news to start. I think you and I both over the past few months have seen public sentiment really begin to turn against AI and this week, unfortunately, we saw that sentiment spill over into violence. Yeah, so most of our listeners have probably heard by now that late last week there was an attempted attack on Sam Altman at his house in San Francisco. A 20-year-old man allegedly threw a Molotov cocktail at the gate of Sam's home. No one was hurt, but according to the criminal complaint against the suspect, this was someone who had a document that identified views opposed to artificial intelligence. He also had a list of names and addresses of other AI executives, investors, and board members. This is someone who was very clearly concerned about the existential risk that AI posed in his opinion, and so decided to take matters into his own hands and go try to attack Sam Altman. From there, he made his way over to OpenAI headquarters to try to commit some violence there as well. And as you say, fortunately no one there was hurt. This incident came just a few days after another really worrisome incident in Indiana. In that incident, an Indianapolis City Councilman named Ron Gibson and his son woke up to more than a dozen gunshots being fired at their front door and a note tucked under their doormat that read "no data centers." This was someone who had been a supporter of a proposed data center in his district in Indiana and had voted to approve rezoning for the project the week before. And I think this is just part of what I am worried is a growing trend of anti-AI radicalization and violence. We should just say like up front, that we are not fans of violence. We do not encourage violence. No one should be doing this. This is very bad for society and even for the sort of proposed policy outcomes of the groups that are most worried about AI. Yeah, I mean in addition to just the moral reasons why it is bad to try to hurt people to achieve a political objective, it also is just very ineffective. No one is going to stop the march of AI with a few stray bullets. So, before we talk about the AI backlash let’s make our AI disclosures. I work for The New York Times which is suing OpenAI, Microsoft, and Perplexity. And my fiance works at Anthropic. You bring up the data center connection in the Indiana case and I want you to lay out some of this backlash that we're seeing to data centers around the country. Data centers are necessary for AI companies to deliver the services that they are building now. Some of the companies right now have a big sort of crunch in trying to deliver as much service as there is currently demand for. But increasingly, we are just seeing people across the country rise up and say, literally, "not in my backyard." Yeah, so I think the data centers and the violence or attempted violence against AI executives share sort of a common fury and outrage. They are obviously very different tactics. I think data centers are kind of the most visible symbol of the AI boom and I think there are a lot of fears and worries and concerns about data center construction out there. Some of them are based on more sound reasons than others, but we have seen not just sort of individual threats against people who support data centers, but also just that there is a lot of political resistance forming in opposition to these data centers. These are people who I think think that this sort of boom is bad, or at least that they don't want it taking place in their neighborhoods. So, describe some of this resistance. The state of Maine recently passed a temporary moratorium that would ban data centers larger than 20 megawatts until November 2027. There’s a suburb of Milwaukee, Wisconsin, Port Washington, which is going to be the home of one of these big OpenAI-Oracle-Stargate data centers. That town recently voted overwhelmingly in favor of restricting the building of future data centers. Basically, you have to get voter approval before you do any of these things. Then there’s also similar efforts going on in places like Ohio, Missouri, Indiana, Georgia, and North Carolina. And there’s also this big federal data center moratorium that has been proposed by Bernie Sanders in the Senate and AOC in the House that would basically put a national moratorium on the construction of data centers. So I think data centers are kind of where this is becoming hottest fastest, but I do think we are seeing also these individual threats on the executives and leaders of these big AI companies by people who just do not think this is headed in a good direction. Yes, and on that point, I want you to describe a little bit some of the broader backdrop here because over the past several weeks, I have seen survey after survey that just says, in one way or another, Americans do not believe that AI is likely to be a positive in their lives. So tell us about some of that data. What you see is kind of a slow turn against the AI industry over the course of really the last year or two. There’s a new report out from Stanford this week, the 2026 version of their AI Index, which sort of catalogs various trends in the AI industry. And basically, their takeaway was that, in the US, people have very low trust in not only AI but on the question of whether their own government can regulate AI in a responsible way. The global average on that question was 54% of like, "do you trust your own government to responsibly regulate AI?" In the US, that's only 31%. The data is a little fuzzier in some other studies. There was a Pew study earlier this year that showed that people's attitudes in the US are more negative than positive when it comes to data centers' impact on the environment, home energy costs, and quality of life nearby. However, more Americans view the economic effects of AI as being potentially more positive. So it's a little fuzzy, sort of depending on how you slice the data and how you ask the questions, but I think it's fair to say that most polls and surveys of public sentiment around AI have shown that people are getting more concerned as these systems get more powerful. Yeah. So let's talk about why we think this turn has happened so dramatically. So what do you make of the idea, Kevin, that a reason for the sort of negative sentiment against the AI companies and the industry at large is being driven by investigative journalism? Yeah, see, I want to ask you about this because I feel like there is a certain bind here that these companies and their leaders are in when it comes to talking about some of the scarier possible outcomes of AI. I think a lot of them watched the social media CEOs claim that their technologies during the last decade would produce nothing but good for the world, right? It was going to connect everyone; it was going to be this glorious utopia. I think a lot of them took the lesson from that that we have to be up front. If we think the thing that we're building has some risk attached to it, like we should be open and honest about that and not sugarcoat it. How do you think they should square that circle? So I think that there is a third path forward here which is to essentially just try to work with the governments and put more pressure on them to put into place systems that would regulate the companies to mitigate the harms caused by their products. Yeah. All right, so we don't seem to think that journalism is the reason that people are so upset with the AI companies. Let me propose a second answer: economic worries. I feel like when you and I are out there talking to most folks, to the extent that people have a concrete near-term worry about AI, it is that it is either going to totally replace their job or it is just going to make the job that they have now horrible. How does that sound as an explanation to you? Yeah, I am sort of more in line with this view of things that, sort of in the same way that all politics is local, I think that all AI politics or most AI politics ultimately come down to people just sort of looking at a technology and thinking "what will this do to me and my ability to continue to live my life and support my family and, you know, retire comfortably?" Like I think people have a lot of fears about this stuff. And that is, I think, a bigger part of the sort of data center opposition. I don't think a lot of the people opposing data centers are worried about existential risk; I think they're mostly focused on like, "this thing seems super annoying and maybe it's going to take my job and pollute my environment." And I think people are saying, "Wait a minute, we're supposed to be rooting for this? Like, why would I root for something that might make it harder for me to put food on the table?" I really do think that is the skeleton key that unlocks some vast portion of the entire AI debate, right? And it doesn't seem to be something that so far any of the AI companies have really had an answer for. Now, if you sort of, you know, keep them up late at night and have one of these like dorm room conversations, they will describe for you visions of fully automated luxury communism where you don't have to have a job anymore and neither does anyone in your family. And they say all of your meals will be 3D printed for you and it won't cost you a thing. I think that just seems so implausible to people that it's impossible to build any kind of political constituency around it. I wonder if we will see AI companies trying to sell that case a little bit harder in the future, right? Yeah, I mean I think this is the biggest cultural disconnect between the San Francisco-Silicon Valley AI bubble and the rest of the country. I think people here, many of the people I talk to, they are excited about a period of rapid technological change. That is what excites them, they're motivated by making it happen, they think ultimately this will be a good thing for society. I think most people don't think like that. They don't think, "I want to live through a period of unprecedented technological change in which the world becomes unrecognizable to me." I think there are a lot of people trying to send that message by opposing data centers, but I don't think it's really sunk in at the AI companies or to the people running them that most people want stability in their lives. They want to be able to plan for their futures. And when people from Silicon Valley show up and say "hey, we've got this amazing new technology and by the way it might take away your job and there's nothing you can do about it," I think that naturally breeds some fear and resentment. So this gets into the third explanation for what I think is going on here which I want to put under the broad label of anti-elitism, right? This AI moment that we're living through is a top-down moment. It did not rise up from the grassroots, from a bunch of nerds getting together in their garages and training frontier models. It was a small group of really smart people who were able to get access to massive amounts of capital from the elites in our society and they are now mounting this effort to build it very quickly, deploy it very quickly without a lot of guardrails. And so I think when the average person looks at this, they think, "not only did I not ask for this, but I have no meaningful control over it, right?" I think that is a big reason that you're seeing people so furious because, particularly on the left, this just looks like a mostly right-wing elite project that is being championed by President Trump and the many venture capitalists that are in his administration. If you're already worried that it's going to take your job and you don't feel like you have any control over it, well of course you're going to hate it. I don't think this is some like elite right-wing plot but it is definitely an elitist project that is being undertaken by a very small handful of people who are not elected, who don't have all that much accountability. And I think that is in part by design. I think these people have seen that when you do give the public a right to weigh in on how technology is deployed, they mostly vote to stop it, right? As we're seeing now with these data centers, as we're seeing around the country with the backlash against Waymo, which we haven't even talked about but this has been truly surprising to me. Waymo has now a technology in its self-driving cars that is demonstrably safer than human drivers and you would think that that would be greeted as kind of an unambiguous good. Yet, in a lot of places where they're trying to expand, people are saying "no no no no no no, think of all the jobs we'll lose if this technology comes in." Now I think in the tech industry, that attitude is mostly sort of mocked and dismissed as like "oh, like we're literally showing up here with a life-saving technology and you're saying 'what about the taxi drivers?'" And I think there’s a cohort of people in Silicon Valley, many of whom we talk to and know, who just think like this technology is too important to be left to the masses. And I think that is like a misguided attitude, but it is definitely an attitude that is out there. Yeah. I mean I do think it is really misguided because it's one thing to say, "well, we cannot trust the public to vote correctly about how new technologies could be deployed." Did JD Vance Just Blow Up His 2028 Run? | Pivot

Pivot with Kara Swisher and Scott Galloway(233.0K) ·54:35 ·60.8K views · 2026-04-17
Kara Swisher (Co-host, Pivot) Scott Galloway (Co-host, Pivot) JD Vance (Vice President of the United States)
> "The stock market is now a proxy for companies that are mostly invested in technology and some oil companies...
TL;DR: While global geopolitical tensions escalate, the stock market remains dislocated from consumer reality; meanwhile, Anthropic has overtaken OpenAI in revenue momentum as Amazon positions itself to challenge Starlink’s satellite dominance. Conflict in the Middle East:...

TL;DR: While global geopolitical tensions escalate, the stock market remains dislocated from consumer reality; meanwhile, Anthropic has overtaken OpenAI in revenue momentum as Amazon positions itself to challenge Starlink’s satellite dominance.

Geopolitical Tensions and Market Dislocation

  • Conflict in the Middle East: Despite Iranian threats to the Red Sea and a U.S. blockade of Iranian ports, markets remain resilient.
      • Strait of Hormuz: Scott Galloway supports a U.S. threat to block the Strait entirely to maintain the principle of freedom of navigation, noting that allowing regional powers to block global transit sets a dangerous precedent.
  • Economic Decoupling: The S&P 500 rose 2.8% during the taping, reflecting a "dislocation" between market performance and the lived experience of average Americans.
      • The Wealth Gap: The top decile of Americans now owns 90% of stocks and is responsible for 50% of consumer spending.
      • Inflationary Pressures: While the wealthy remain unaffected, the lower half of the country is struggling with gasoline prices (up 36%) and Brent crude (up 32%).
  • Energy Independence: U.S. energy independence has allowed the domestic market to ignore oil shocks that are currently devastating European benchmarks.

The AI Power Shift: Anthropic vs. OpenAI

  • Revenue Momentum: Anthropic has reportedly tripled its Annual Recurring Revenue (ARR) in just four months, growing from $9 billion to $30 billion.
      • OpenAI Lagging: OpenAI’s ARR stands at approximately $25 billion, marking the first time Anthropic has led on revenue.
  • Enterprise vs. Consumer Strategy: 80% of Anthropic’s revenue is enterprise-driven, which Galloway argues is a more stable and higher-margin business than OpenAI’s 40% enterprise share.
  • The Microsoft Fracture: OpenAI is publicly distancing itself from Microsoft, claiming the partnership limits its ability to meet enterprise needs, while "bear-hugging" Amazon.
      • Leadership Critique: Galloway suggests OpenAI is "burning up on re-entry," citing public tiffs with Microsoft and a 27% chance (via prediction markets) that Sam Altman will be replaced as CEO by year-end.

Amazon’s $11.5B Satellite Play

  • Globalstar Acquisition: Amazon is acquiring satellite operator Globalstar to bolster "Project LEO" (formerly Project Kuiper), aiming to become the "strongest infrastructure company in the world."
      • Catching Starlink: Amazon currently has 240 operational satellites compared to Starlink’s 10,000+. Amazon plans to deploy 3,200 by 2029.
      • Strategic Infrastructure: Beyond consumer Wi-Fi, the network will support Amazon’s internal B2B needs, including warehouses, delivery hubs, and autonomous drones.
  • "Amazon Prime Plus": Galloway predicts Amazon will eventually launch a phone and a "Prime Plus" tier, offering lightning-fast home and mobile connectivity to its 115 million subscriber households.

Political and Corporate Maneuvering

  • Airlines Merger: United Airlines CEO Scott Kirby has reportedly pitched a merger with American Airlines directly to Donald Trump.
      • Market Concentration: A combined entity would control 40% of U.S. domestic capacity. Galloway calls the proposal "antitrust nightmare" and "ridiculous," noting it would leave only one or two carriers on 289 overlapping routes.
  • Jerome Powell vs. Trump: Trump continues to threaten to fire Fed Chair Jerome Powell, despite Powell's term lasting until 2028. Galloway views these attacks as "distractions" from negative news cycles.
  • JD Vance and the Pope: Vice President Vance criticized the Pope for speaking on "theology" regarding peace. Swisher and Galloway noted this is a "politically stupid" move that risks alienating swing-voting Evangelicals and Catholics.

Financial Policy and Predictions

  • Trump "Baby Bonds": Five million children have signed up for Trump accounts featuring $1,000 seed deposits. Galloway supports the concept of "baby bonds" but suggests they should be locked until age 65 to eventually replace Social Security.
  • Prediction: The "AI Pivot" Bubble: Galloway predicts a wave of failing companies will rebrand as AI firms to trigger short-term stock pops.
      • Case Study: Allbirds (rebranded as NewBird AI) saw a 900% intraday spike after announcing a pivot to leasing GPUs. Galloway warns that copycats like Chegg or GameStop may follow, leading to significant retail investor losses.

***

Notable Quotes

"The stock market is now a proxy for companies that are mostly invested in technology and some oil companies... wealthy people just aren't affected [by gas prices]." — Scott Galloway
"I think he’s [JD Vance] torpedoing his 2026 chances by picking a fight [with the Pope]... the Pope is talking about peace, he’s talking about tyrants, he’s talking about everything Jesus talked about." — Kara Swisher

Bank Earnings Are In: Here’s What They’re REALLY Saying About the U.S. Economy | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(169.0K) ·25:38 ·7.6K views · 2026-04-17
Steve Eisman (Investor)
> "As the banks go, so goes the economy.
TL;DR: Despite fears of a private credit downturn, latest earnings from major banks show "benign" credit quality and a resilient U.S. economy, suggesting no imminent recession even as AI disruption looms over the software sector. Credit Quality Benchmarks: To gauge the...

TL;DR: Despite fears of a private credit downturn, latest earnings from major banks show "benign" credit quality and a resilient U.S. economy, suggesting no imminent recession even as AI disruption looms over the software sector.

State of the U.S. Credit Cycle & Economy

  • Credit Quality Benchmarks: To gauge the economy, Eisman focuses on "non-accruing loans" (loans delinquent by 90+ days) at the four largest banks.
      • J.P. Morgan: Non-accruals were $9.6 billion, up 11% year-over-year but down 3% sequentially from Q4.
      • Bank of America: Total non-accruals were $5.8 billion, down 4% year-over-year and flat compared to Q4.
  • Economic Resilience: There is currently no data supporting a broad credit cycle or recession. While the economy is "K-shaped" (not fine for everyone), the banking data suggests the overall U.S. economy remains powerful.
  • Geopolitical Impact: Despite the breakdown of U.S.-Iran talks and the blocking of the Strait of Hormuz, markets remain optimistic about a settlement. Oil briefly spiked above $100 but retreated, and the S&P 500 has returned to record highs.

Major Bank Earnings Analysis

  • Investment-Centric Outperformance: Diversified firms (Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, JPM) generally outperformed lending-centric banks (Wells Fargo, Bank of America).
      • Morgan Stanley: Labeled "best-in-class" this quarter with a 27% return on tangible common equity (ROTCE), the highest in the industry.
      • Goldman Sachs: Reported a massive 21% ROTCE, driven by an 89% year-over-year increase in advisory revenue, though fixed income trading was a "disappointment" (down 10%).
      • Citigroup: Showing signs of a turnaround under Jane Fraser with a 13% ROTCE, its best in years, leading to a valuation re-rating to 1.3x tangible book value.
  • Lending Headwinds:
      • Wells Fargo: Disappointed the market with a 13-basis point sequential decline in net interest margin. Net interest income (NII) remains a primary driver of earnings for lending-heavy banks.
      • Valuation Gap: Lending-centric banks trade at lower multiples (1.8x–1.9x tangible book) compared to investment-heavy banks (~3x tangible book) because lending is highly competitive and net interest margins are in long-term decline.

Private Credit & Regional Bank Risks

  • Regional Bank Exposure: Contrary to some market fears, regional banks (represented by the KRE ETF) have very little exposure to private credit.
  • Concentrated Risk: Private credit exposure is concentrated in the 32 largest financial institutions.
      • Disclosed Exposures: J.P. Morgan ($50B), Wells Fargo ($36B), and Citi ($22B).
      • Subordination Protections: Most bank loans to the private credit sector are senior with roughly 40% loss cushions, meaning the underlying assets must lose significant value before the banks suffer capital hits.
  • New Opportunities: Firms like Blackstone and Goldman are raising new $10B funds. These are attractive because they are "clean"—they lack the problematic software loan exposure found in older private credit portfolios.

Emerging Risks: AI and Software

  • Structural Threat: AI poses a multi-faceted threat to the software sector that may take over a year to clarify.
      • Decreased Barriers: Concerns that AI will allow customers to build internal custom software rather than purchasing external solutions.
      • Pricing Power: Potential for AI to permanently erode the ability of software companies to raise prices.
  • Investment Strategy: Eisman advises "treading carefully" with the software group. It is difficult to predict a bottom when the narrative has turned so aggressively negative.

Notable Quotes

"As the banks go, so goes the economy. Until credit quality changes, the U.S. economy will be fine." — Steve Eisman
"Lending is highly, highly competitive... as a result, net interest margins have been in long-term decline for years and that has put pressure on returns." — Steve Eisman
"If and when losses appear [in private credit], this will be an income statement problem, but not a systemic capital problem." — Steve Eisman

We did this episode specifically to shed as much light as possible on how and when this conflict will end and how long the consequences will continue impacting the economy and markets. Now, at the end of the first quarter, the S&P was down 4% for the year, and NASDAQ was down 7%. Both are now up on the year and are at record highs. I think the market is assuming the war will be over very soon. Whether that's true or not, we will have to see. Moving on to stocks, the banks kicked off earnings season. I'm going to share some thoughts on a bunch of the large banks that reported and then show you how to think about valuation given the results. But before I do that, I first want to focus on how the large bank results are of particular importance because they provide great insights into the state of the current credit cycle. The four large banks that reported—J.P. Morgan, Citi, Wells, and Bank of America—have large and broad-based lending businesses. On the consumer side, they do credit cards, auto loans, and several other types of consumer loans. On the commercial side, they make commercial real estate and corporate loans and other types of commercial loans as well. They don't do everything, but they are big enough and broad enough to provide a real window into the health of the US economy. By analyzing the credit results of these four large banks, we can get a closer understanding of whether the problems in the private credit sector are broadening into a larger credit cycle for the overall economy. As you all know, there is growing fear that credit losses will start to mount for the first time since the great financial crisis. We've had 17 years of amazingly benign credit quality and investors are wondering if the good times are over. Yes, private credit is exposed to software, no question. And software is getting hit hard by AI. But are there signs of broad credit problems emerging in the actual data? When the major banks report, they show reams of data. J.P. Morgan's earnings release supplement is 28 pages long and is filled with data on every page. That is typical of how the large banks report. To see trends in credit quality, the best place to look is to examine consumer and commercial non-accruing loans. A non-accruing loan is a loan that is seriously delinquent, usually defined as 90 plus days. A bank no longer reports interest income from these loans and also reserves for future losses. I am putting on the screen consumer and commercial non-accruing loan data for J.P. Morgan, Wells Fargo, Citi, and Bank of America for 1Q25, 4Q25, and 1Q26—the quarter just reported. These four banks combined provide a large window into credit quality trends. If there was a serious trend of deteriorating credit, we would see increases in non-accruing consumer or commercial loans on a year-over-year basis and a quarterly sequential basis. We certainly saw large increases in consumer non-accruals leading up to the great financial crisis. But we are not seeing that now. The credit data is fairly benign in both consumer and commercial. For those of you who are audio listeners, I'll just focus on J.P. Morgan and Bank of America. For J.P. Morgan, total non-accruing loans were 9.6 billion in 1Q26. They were up 11% year-over-year, but down 3% versus the fourth quarter. The numbers were even more benign at Bank of America. 1Q total non-accruals for Bank of America were 5.8 billion, down 4% year-over-year and flat with the fourth quarter. The credit trends reported by the large banks are very benign. Now, maybe a credit cycle will emerge, but we are not yet seeing it in the banks. And that is a very important data point. The banks have the broadest credit exposures, so their results are a concurrent indicator. As the banks go, so goes the economy. And this brings me to my conclusion about the state of the US economy. Since the great financial crisis, many commentators have tried to predict the next crisis. "This is it," they say, and then it isn't. Perhaps the most important story of the past 17 years is how powerful and resilient the US economy has become. Yes, there are problems in private credit with respect to overexposure to software, no question. And perhaps these software issues are harbingers of an approaching credit cycle. Yet the banks have shown once again that credit quality in the US is okay. Until that changes, the US economy will be fine. Fine, yes, but we do have a K-shaped economy, so it's not fine for everyone. That's a very important point to emphasize. But I just don't see a recession on the horizon as long as bank credit quality is benign. By the way, partially because of the benign credit statistics reported by the banks this week, the entire private equity and private credit sector rallied this week. This earnings season, the large banks also provided more data on their exposures to private credit. Wells disclosed 36 billion in exposure to private credit with companies in business services, software, and healthcare industries making up about half of that amount. Software in particular accounts for 17% of the total, or 6 billion. Most of the loans, however, have about a 40% cushion, meaning that the loans would have to suffer losses greater than 40% before Wells suffers any losses. That's not bad. However, Wells is having issues here. It is a lender to Market Financial Solutions, which is a fraud, and is also a lender to Goeasy, a Canadian non-prime lender that is having problems. On its conference call in response to a question, Jamie Dimon said that J.P. Morgan's exposure to private credit is 50 billion, and Citi reported 22 billion of such loans. Now let's talk about the earnings results. As far as the bank earnings results are concerned, the banks with large investment banking and trading business, which is Goldman, Morgan Stanley, J.P. Morgan, and Citi, did better generally than the banks that are more lending-centric, Wells Fargo and Bank of America. Let's start with Goldman. Goldman's first quarter for 2026, they reported 17.55 in earnings per share versus the street of 16.52, so a beat. It was up an impressive 24% versus last year. They had a very impressive 21% return on tangible common equity. It was a solid beat thanks to higher revenue and a much lower tax rate. Higher revenue was mostly due to a huge advisory quarter, which was up 89% year-over-year. There was strong equity trading and good results in asset management. However, there was no operating leverage as revenue and expenses were both up 14%. Also, the tax rate was only 13% versus the usual 21%, and that's not a recurring event. So all in all, a good quarter, but expectations were high, so the stock was down on the print. Perhaps it was down because fixed income trading at Goldman was a disappointment and was down 10% versus last year. Goldman's fixed income trading results were a bit strange in that all the other major banks reported strong trading results. I have to say that over the years I have given up trying to figure out how the market will react to a Goldman print. There is just no way to predict it. Let's go to J.P. Morgan. Very good quarter. They reported earnings of 5.94, while the street estimate was 5.51. The company generated a very strong 23% return on tangible common equity. Earnings per share and revenue beat. Highlights were a very strong fixed income trading revenue result, up 21% year-over-year, as opposed to the 10% decline at Goldman, and positive operating leverage. Jamie Dimon made plenty of cautionary comments about credit during the conference call. Boy, does he love to do that. But the bank's credit data was quite benign. Moving on to Wells Fargo. Wells unfortunately has a tendency to report some not-great quarters every now and then. And this was one of those times. 1Q net interest income was weaker than estimates on a sequential net interest margin decline of 13 basis points. The company would have missed earnings but for a lower-than-normal tax rate. Thus, while profits were up 7% year-over-year, much of that increase was from lower taxes. The market does not like it when Wells or any bank for that matter misses on net interest income. And the stock was down pretty good on Tuesday. I'd also note that a 13-basis point sequential decline in net interest margin for a bank is actually quite large, and changes in the net interest margin are by far the largest driver of earnings for a lending-focused bank like Wells. Moving on to Citi. Good quarter. Citi reported earnings of 3.06, while the street estimate was 2.63. It was a strong start to the year for Citi with a 13% return on tangible common equity. This was driven by 14% growth in revenues versus 7% growth in expenses. So there was nice operating leverage. Trading was good, investment banking was good, and wealth management was pretty good. The credit data was quite benign. Morgan Stanley: 1Q earnings of 3.43 versus the street of 3.02 and 32% year-over-year growth. I would call this a best-in-class quarter. Everything did well and the company produced a 27% return on tangible common equity, which is the highest in the industry. Underwriting was a bit softer than the street, but advisory was strong, trading was strong, and wealth management was good. And finally, Bank of America reported earnings of a dollar eleven versus the street of a dollar two. That's 23% year-over-year growth. Pretty good for Bank of America. It was a solid quarter with a little bit better net interest income than people were looking for. There was good advisory and equity trading, and a lower provision with benign credit data. Most importantly, for a few years now, Bank of America's return on tangible common equity has been stuck at around 14%. This quarter, that was elevated to 16%. So that's quite good for Bank of America. It is better than Wells, but still below the banks like Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, and J.P. Morgan which are even more capital market-centric. Now let's turn to how to think about the valuation of the banks. I am putting on the screen a table for the major banks that shows the quarter's return on tangible common equity, the actual dollar amount of tangible common equity per share, and the price of the stock divided by that tangible common equity per share. The general rule of thumb is that a bank with a consistently high return on tangible common equity will have a higher price divided by tangible common equity than other banks. Generally, the banks with large investment banking, trading, and asset management businesses yield higher returns than their more lending-centric competitors. That's why Goldman, Morgan Stanley, and J.P. Morgan have higher return on tangible common equities and therefore higher price to tangible common equity valuations. Notice, and I'll say this for those who can't see it, that Goldman and J.P. Morgan are slightly below three times tangible book value. Morgan Stanley, with the best-in-class return on tangible common equity of 27%, is above three times. Compare that to Wells at 1.8 times and Bank of America at 1.9 times. Goldman Sachs, J.P. Morgan, and Morgan Stanley all have returns above 20%, while Wells and BofA are at 14 to 16%. The obvious question to ask is, why do lending-centric banks have lower returns than the banks with more diversified businesses? The answer is that lending is highly, highly competitive. Banks compete for loans with other banks and with a multitude of non-banks as well. As a result, net interest margins have been in long-term decline for years and that has put pressure on returns. A further word on Goldman and Citi. First, Goldman. Over the past few years, Goldman has completely re-rated. A few years ago, Goldman was valued at only 1.3 times tangible book value. But as the capital markets and M&A environment improved, returns improved dramatically and the stock got re-valued higher to its current nearly three times tangible book value. That is quite a change. When Goldman was at 1.3 times tangible, it was a value stock. Buying it now at these levels is just a bet that the good times will continue. And with respect to Citi. Since the GFC, Citi has had the worst return profile of all the large banks by far. Its return on tangible common equity was consistently below 10%, which stinks, and the stock was valued below tangible book value. CEO Jane Fraser has done a very good job; she has streamlined the bank and improved its return profile. This quarter's 13% return on tangible common equity is the best return level in years. That is why Citi is now valued at 1.3 times tangible as opposed to what it used to be which was below tangible. That is still a steep discount to the other large banks, but it is moving in the right direction. And now for the mailbag. First question is from Antonio who asks: "Hi Steve, hope you are well. You have been talking about private credit for a while and the situation has been unraveling. What do you think of regional banks in the US in that context? How exposed are they and how much could this hurt them? Do you think a short position in the KRE ETF is a good way to hedge against the private credit issue or is the exposure in regional banks not that large?" Regional banks do not have the lion's share of exposure to private credit. In particular, the regional banks in the KRE ETF have actually very little exposure to private credit. The exposure is in the larger banks. Now, banks provide a great deal of data in what are called call reports. The category in question is loans to non-depository financial institutions, otherwise known as NDFIs. And loans to NDFIs are divided up into one, mortgages; two, business credit; three, private equity; four, consumer; and five, other. It appears that private credit loans are in the business credit category, but it is unclear what percentage of that entire category is private credit. NDFI loan exposure is not evenly distributed across all the banks. It is confined to the 32 largest institutions. The private equity exposure is 315 billion and the total business credit exposure is 335 billion, but again it is unclear how much of that 335 billion is to private credit. I suspect that at least 50% of the 335 is to private credit, but again that is just a guess. This quarter, as I discussed, Citi, J.P. Morgan, and Wells all provided their total exposures to private credit. J.P. Morgan is 50 billion. In the context of the overall size of J.P. Morgan, that is not that large. I feel similarly with respect to Citi and Wells. But again, the banks in the KRE regional bank index do not have much exposure to private credit. My view about the banks is that if and when private credit experiences losses, the banks with exposure will have some losses too. However, many of the bank loans to private credit are senior loans where there is subordination protection underneath. So it is unclear how much in the way of losses the banks will actually suffer. I also believe that the banks are very well-capitalized. So if and when losses appear, this will be an income statement problem, but not a systemic capital problem. As of now, however, bank earnings season indicates that the credit cycle is not yet here. Second question comes from Amit, who asks: "Dear Steve, I am a big fan of your show, I listen to it weekly and it assists me very much in understanding markets, more than any other finance channel out there. I would be happy to hear your perspective on High Yield, current spreads, credit quality and how it relates if at all to direct private lending issues out there. Thank you and all the best. Amit." Thank you for the compliment. Credit spreads are a measurement of risk. All fixed income products are priced off the Treasury yield curve because Treasuries are considered to be the risk-free rate. All non-US Treasury debt has a higher yield than Treasuries of the same duration. The difference between the yield on a non-Treasury form of debt and Treasuries is called the credit spread. When credit spreads are tight or narrow, that is an indication that investors are optimistic and unafraid of risk. When investors start to worry about credit quality or worry about anything in fact, credit spreads widen. Widening credit spreads do not necessarily mean that losses are around the corner. It can just be that investors are more afraid. Because of the bad news surrounding private credit, wide credit spreads can be attractive to investors as it means that future loans will have higher yields and could be more lucrative. And credit spreads have been widening throughout fixed income including high yield, thereby making high yield loans in particular appear to be more attractive. That is also why Blackstone and Goldman have both recently raised 10 billion dollar private credit funds from institutional investors. Those new funds are not burdened by exposure to problematic software loans because they'll be making new loans. That's why these new funds don't help existing investors in private credit. As for predicting this credit cycle, I think, and I've said this before, it's just too early to make any firm decisions. The news flow is bad in private credit, but given the earnings reports of the large banks just this week, credit quality overall is still fine. And our last question is from Jacob who asks: "Hi Steve—I love your podcast. Thank you for doing it. Like many people, I have questions about private credit, AI, and software companies. Here's a question I have for you: everyone seems to be talking about how AI will increase competition in the software sector by decreasing the barriers to entry, but what strikes me is how easy it will be for software customers to actually just build their own internal, custom software solutions. If that's true, then it's not just a question of how much competition will increase, but by how much demand will decrease. If we were to think about it that way, would that change the narrative? How much and in what ways? And, of course, what should retail investors do because of it?" This last Monday, April 13th, we hosted Rob Oliver, the software analyst at Baird, and discussed many of these issues. It was a great interview and everyone should check that out. But to summarize, right now investors are worried about everything when it comes to the AI threat to software. They are worried that AI companies will replace existing software platforms. They are worried that the cost of creating software will decline to such a degree that more software work will be done internally as opposed to purchasing the solution from an outside vendor. They are worried that existing software companies won't be able to raise prices ever again. It's a very long list. It's so early in the story that it's just impossible to know what will happen. I don't think we will have any degree of clarity for at least a year. That's why my advice is to tread carefully with respect to the software group. It's brutal to try to predict the bottom in a group where the narrative has become so negative.
