OpenAI's Identity Crisis, Datacenter Wars, Market Up on Iran News, Mamdani's First Tax, Swalwell Out

All-In Podcast(1.1M) ·90:57 ·13.1K views · 2026-04-17
Travis Kalanick (Entrepreneur and Guest) Eric Swalwell (Former U.S. Representative)
> "Growth is king right now...
TL;DR: OpenAI is facing an "identity crisis" and strategic pivot toward enterprise as Anthropic’s 10x growth rate threatens its dominance, while the tech industry confronts a rising tide of populism targeting data centers and luxury assets. Competitive Escalation: OpenAI’s...

TL;DR: OpenAI is facing an "identity crisis" and strategic pivot toward enterprise as Anthropic’s 10x growth rate threatens its dominance, while the tech industry confronts a rising tide of populism targeting data centers and luxury assets.

OpenAI’s Identity Crisis and the Enterprise Pivot

  • Competitive Escalation: OpenAI’s Chief Revenue Officer, Denise Dresser, issued a leaked four-page memo attacking Anthropic’s valuation and revenue metrics.
      • Revenue Discrepancies: The memo claims Anthropic’s $30 billion run rate is inflated by ~$8 billion through revenue-sharing agreements with cloud providers.
      • Growth Rates: David Sacks noted that while OpenAI is growing at 3–4x annually, Anthropic is growing at 10x, potentially reaching an $80–$100 billion run rate by year-end.
  • Strategic Shift: OpenAI is pivoting aggressively toward business customers and the "agent platform layer."
      • Enterprise Focus: The move is intended to capture scalable revenue from coding and enterprise tokens, which offer higher margins than the $20/month consumer subscription model.
      • Codex vs. Claude: Chamath Palihapitiya noted that OpenAI’s "Codex" remains superior for complex, long-horizon coding tasks, even as Anthropic’s "Claude" gains ground in day-to-day reliability.
  • Valuation Concerns: Investors expressed frustration with OpenAI's lack of focus. With a recent $122 billion funding round, the company may need a $1.2 trillion IPO valuation for the math to work for current backers.

The Datacenter Wars and Rising Populism

  • Infrastructure Backlash: Data centers have become the new "attack vector" for populist resentment.
      • Symbolism of Wealth: David Friedberg argued that data centers represent the "temples of the wealthy," becoming physical targets for those who feel left behind by the AI boom.
      • Regulatory Resistance: Sacks noted that data centers are being contested in approximately 30 states due to concerns over energy consumption and "doomer NIMBYism."
  • The Ratepayer Protection Pledge: To combat the narrative that data centers drive up local electricity costs, major tech players are increasingly required to "Bring Their Own Energy" (BYOE) or remain power-neutral to the grid.
  • Sovereign Compute: Sacks highlighted the strategic importance of building data centers in the Gulf (GCC), noting that the IRGC has threatened these sites because they are seen as critical American strategic assets.

The "Democratic Machine" and Swalwell’s Exit

  • Coordinated Resignation: Eric Swalwell’s exit from the California governor’s race and resignation from Congress was characterized as a "lancing of the boil" by the Democratic establishment.
      • The "Pelosi Factor": Sacks compared the exit to Joe Biden’s withdrawal, suggesting Nancy Pelosi likely gave Swalwell the "hard way or easy way" ultimatum to prevent a fragmented primary from handing the race to a Republican.
      • Oppo Research Timing: Friedberg revealed he heard rumors of the allegations against Swalwell as early as December, suggesting the "Democratic Machine" held the information until it was politically advantageous to force a withdrawal.
  • Congressional Trading: The panel noted that Ro Khanna has traded over $600 million in stock, outperforming even Nancy Pelosi. Chamath argued that the lack of Reg FD (Fair Disclosure) for Congress allows members to trade on information gathered in committee meetings.

NYC’s Pied-à-Terre Tax and Market Indicators

  • The "Mamdani Tax": New York is considering a 3.9% annual tax on second homes (pied-à-terres) valued over $5 million.
      • Market Impact: Sacks argued this will crash the luxury market and deter investment, as the tax target is the "most elastic" part of the market.
      • Capital Flight: Travis Kalanick and Chamath compared the move to London’s "stamp tax," which caused a "slow bleed" of capital to more hospitable cities like Zurich or Milan.
  • Market Outlook: The market is currently showing confounding signals.
      • Valuation Metrics: Both the "Buffett Index" (Equity/GDP) and the Shiller PE ratio are at historical peaks, suggesting a "risk-off" environment.
      • AI Efficiency: While the panel is bullish on AI’s long-term potential, Chamath warned that large enterprises have yet to see "tsunamis of revenue" from AI, calling current agent outputs "slop" that requires significant human-in-the-loop refinement.
"Growth is king right now... if Anthropic is growing faster than OpenAI by a significant clip, the investors are going to play it forward. Efficiency will outstrip subsidy; you can't keep raising $100 billion rounds forever." — Travis Kalanick
"The data center is the pied-à-terre of this space. It is the manifestation that people want to attack and destroy because they don't see the value in AI for their own lives yet." — David Friedberg
"Nancy Pelosi went to the President and said, 'We can do things the hard way or the easy way.' I think the same thing happened to Swalwell. He was too dumb to listen, and they did things the hard way." — David Sacks
"I'm generally more risk-off right now... I think it is crucial that the SpaceX IPO get done ASAP, and that one of Anthropic or OpenAI front-run the other to get out first." — Chamath Palihapitiya

Why your CEO mentality is wrong (with investor Byron Deeter) | Masters of Scale

Masters of Scale(154.0K) ·37:14 ·153 views · 2026-04-17
Byron Deeter (Investor, Bessemer Venture Partners)
Scaling a world-class company requires preserving the "founder magic" through long-term leadership while adopting a "pro-athlete" mentality toward executive health and prioritizing high-conviction "big swings" in transformative sectors like AI.
TL;DR Scaling a world-class company requires preserving the "founder magic" through long-term leadership while adopting a "pro-athlete" mentality toward executive health and prioritizing high-conviction "big swings" in transformative sectors like AI. The "Trigo" Inflection...

TL;DR

Scaling a world-class company requires preserving the "founder magic" through long-term leadership while adopting a "pro-athlete" mentality toward executive health and prioritizing high-conviction "big swings" in transformative sectors like AI.

The Founder’s Path and the "Operator-Investor" Balance

  • The "Trigo" Inflection Point: Byron Deeter co-founded Trigo Technologies in 1999, betting on cloud computing when it was highly controversial.
      • Customer Validation: The turning point occurred when "hard, smart customers" (like Staples) provided public testimonials, proving the cloud model worked.
      • Strategic Acquisition: Trigo reached a $50M revenue run rate before being acquired by IBM in 2004, which Deeter describes as one of the largest enterprise software outcomes of that era.
  • The Myth of the "Professional CEO": While Deeter stepped aside for a CEO at Trigo, he now advises most founders *against* this.
      • Founder Magic: Unique product insight is irreplaceable; replacing a founder is destabilizing and introduces significant risk.
      • Ideal Structure: Investors should "hire everyone around the founder" to support their strengths rather than replacing them.
  • The Hippocratic Oath of Venture: As an investor, Deeter follows a "do no harm" policy.
      • Avoid Over-Operating: Investors should not try to fix "okay" companies but instead help "great" companies stay excellent.
      • High-Velocity Communication: Deeter prioritizes low-friction, asynchronous communication (WhatsApp, Slack, Signal) over long meetings to stay in the flow of the CEO's day.

Investment Strategy and the "Anti-Portfolio"

  • Crimes of Omission: Deeter notes that for top-tier firms, the "missed" deals (like Tesla or Atlassian) hurt more than the investments that fail.
  • The Anti-Portfolio: Bessemer publicly lists the great companies they passed on to maintain humility and learn from mistakes.
  • Long-Term Horizons: Bessemer’s average hold period is approximately 14 years. Deeter seeks "Century Companies"—iconic firms that "dent the space-time continuum."
  • Investment Criteria: He looks for two primary (and often conflicting) traits in founders:
      • Intense Conviction: A bold, unwavering vision.
      • Coachability: The ability to seek outbound input when intensity mounts rather than turning inward.

The AI Transformation: Anthropic and Beyond

  • The New Hyperscalers: Deeter believes foundation models (like Anthropic) are the new infrastructure, similar to how AWS/Azure were for the first cloud wave.
  • Anthropic Bet: Bessemer’s investment in Anthropic—their largest check ever—was driven by:
      • Talent Magnetism: CEO Dario Amodei's ability to attract the world's best AI researchers.
      • Ethical AI as a Business Moat: Their commitment to safety and ethics serves as a magnet for enterprise customers who need a trusted partner.
  • The "One-Person Billion-Dollar Company": Deeter predicts we will see a billion-dollar company (on its merits, not just financing) with a single employee within "one year and one day."

CEO Performance: The STRIVE Program

  • CEO as Athlete: Deeter argues that CEOs often treat their bodies poorly, which is "statistically the same as running a company drunk" due to sleep deprivation.
  • The STRIVE Framework: Bessemer launched a program focused on sleep, training, and regimen to apply peak-performance principles from the NFL to tech founders.
      • Mental Health: Deeter emphasizes normalizing therapy and coaching to prevent "tapping out" under the unprecedented stress of the current market.
      • The "Power Bar" Fallacy: Reflecting on his own days as a CEO (where he ended up with 13 cavities from living on protein bars), Deeter advocates for better sleep and nutrition as high-ROI business strategies.

Notable Quotes

"You actually want to seek out the hardest, smartest customers and have them beat the crap out of you because others will follow. Feedback is a gift, and when you don't get hard feedback is when you actually need to worry." — Byron Deeter
"Hippocratic oath of venture: do no harm. Stay the hell out of the way when things are working... Don't try to give product input out of cycle." — Byron Deeter
"Our average hold period is like 14 years... these are long journeys if you're doing really big things. The tiebreaker comes down to the biggest swings and the teams I really want to work with for a decade-plus." — Byron Deeter
"Statistically, it's often the same as driving drunk or running a company drunk when you're sleep-deprived and underperforming. We want our CEOs to treat their bodies with the same science as elite athletes." — Byron Deeter
This is Masters of Scale. I'm your host, Jeff Berman. This week on the show, Byron Deeter. After founding and exiting a pioneering company in the early days of cloud software, Byron moved on to become an iconic investor. He spent 20 years at Bessemer Venture Partners with an incredible portfolio that includes 26 companies each worth more than a billion dollars. We talked about his advice for both founders and investors in this moment of AI transformation, why CEOs need to think more like athletes, and much, much more. Byron, welcome to Masters of Scale. Great to be here. I'm thrilled to have you. You are an investor who started as an operator, so I'd like to start with your operating days. How did you become a founder? I fumbled my way into it. I've always wanted to be an entrepreneur, starting from invention idea books in my elementary school days to ideating in my McKinsey days with colleagues trying to think of startup ideas. When I got my first pass at venture, I did a lot of brainstorming time on the side and found my two co-founders in that journey, diving into the early world of cloud computing over the winter break of 1999 to 2000. Walked in in early January and quit my first venture job to dive in. I'm not sure that everyone knows there was cloud computing in the late 1990s, early 2000s. Set the scene for us. What was the world like back then? So, Salesforce and NetSuite had been founded within a few months of that. It was highly controversial because download speed, browser capability, etc., were still real bottlenecks. But my CTO, ultimately co-founder, convinced me that there was this other way to do things. But it was a big bet, and I'll tell you nine and a half out of 10 venture capital meetings when I went pitching threw me out essentially after the first few pages. Why is that? They refused to accept that the cloud business model deserved to exist. This idea of forward cost and back-loaded revenue made no sense to most folks and it was highly controversial for many years. Where did your conviction come from in the face of all those "nos"? Customer experience first. And we were convinced that maintaining all these back versions and multiple ports to different software stacks and things was a waste of innovation and drove to a lot of shelf-ware and misuse. And so we did believe that the answer was there, that fundamentally it was better for customers and that the business model would follow. But I mean, we went through layoffs, we went through the dot-com collapse, we had tough financing rounds. It was a tough journey. The company was Trigo. Trigo Technologies. And what was the inflection point for Trigo? So, some of those early enterprise customers who would go on stage and speak about it. And I'll remember Staples, Anne-Marie Keane—a lot of credit to our early sponsor there. This is going back over a decade and a half, but she said, "This is real. Like, I'll go talk about it, I'll tell others this is a better way and I'll share it." The first real customers who were hard on us, they were leaders and once we got through their qualifications, they would go tell others that it worked. That for us was really the inflection point to transition. It's pretty unusual for a customer to call and say, "I really want to get on stage and give you a testimonial." How did that come to happen? So, one of the things I carry through now as an investor trying to coach our companies is it's somewhat counterintuitive, but you actually want to seek out the hardest, smartest customers and have them beat the crap out of you because others will follow. Their peers in the industry know how good they are and learn from them. So, take on the hard first and work with them as design partners, go through it. Feedback's a gift, and when you don't get hard feedback is when you actually need to worry. It was all about, "We need you to make a great product and the expectation is then that you will share it. This is non-proprietary—it's important but not proprietary—and go talk about it." And so setting those rules of engagement up front... and one of the things that we ask our sales execs now across our portfolio: if you can't get a reference out of that customer, what are the issues? And among the early contract terms, that's something front and center because we do believe that it's one of the lowest-cost customer acquisition methods when employed correctly. That changed things for investors with you? It did, although often the macro will trump the micro. And so we executed well but the market went through turmoil and so we had one of the biggest market pullbacks of our lifetime going into that, which created some real headwinds. And fortunately, having been in venture before, I knew enough to raise early and have buffer, so we never had a true extinction-level event. But we had to do some layoffs because we weren't going to have cheap access to capital. We had to pull back, buy some time. We went through gyrations, we went through customer churn at various points because of their bankruptcies and chaos. And so I think entrepreneurs need to expect to go through several cycles in your journey and you need to reserve enough flexibility, capital, etc., so that the macro can't sink your ship. Just to complete the Trigo journey, you ultimately sold the company. How big was the company from a revenue and people perspective at the time you sold it? Yeah, we were up to about 50 million in run rate revenue. Importantly, another inflection point, we brought on Tom Riley as CEO and I stayed on the board and ran business development partnerships and all of that. But another inflection point, he's excellent and a long-time lifelong friend from that. And why did you bring him in? I was 26 and we were building an enterprise business and wanted some more executive air cover. And I disclosed that upfront to our investors that at some point we'd want to bring in a CEO. It was my first time in an operating role. And interestingly, I think it was the right decision for us, but I also think it's the wrong decision for the majority of our founders. Oh, say more. Why? And I actually look to invest in founders who are more interested in going longer into the journey. And that's because I think the founder product insight is irreplaceable. And so much of these businesses today are product-led that we want to back founders who are unique and differentiated there and we want them bought in for the journey. And tapping out and hiring a replacement is hard. It's destabilizing, it introduces risk. If they have someone identified, "We're going to do it together," then that's okay early on, but we want to do it jointly. Or if it's the only option and, you know, as we talk about more current days, we've had a lot of CEOs tap out over the last few years because it's been really hard to be a CEO right now, and that's okay, like we'll go on it together. But we don't want to usually start with that with Plan A. So it's more, to use the overused example, Mark finding his Sheryl that you're talking about when you're talking about scaling up the organization. Absolutely. I think that's where you unlock the superpowers. Design the founder role that is their best and highest use. At a board meeting yesterday, we were talking about this exact thing. As a founding CEO, you've got the luxury of designing your exec team—do it however you want. Like, what things do you enjoy doing? What are you best at? What's going to give you energy and what's going to give you most impact? And then we'll hire everyone around you. But the idea—and again, there are some cases where the founder just says, "Hey, I want to run product and I don't like this business stuff at all. Let's go get someone." But I'd much rather do that up front or really have some people identified than get into something and then a year later the founder's just like, "I'm out," and then you're taking a bunch of risk and you lose that founder magic. It still takes a lot of good ego to acknowledge where you're not strong, to bring in people who are incredibly capable at things you are not, whether you are replacing yourself as CEO or building out your leadership team. What's the signal that you're looking for from founders when you're considering investing as to whether they have that trait? Yeah, two things that in some ways may be at odds, but it's intense conviction and coachability. And the way that they're at odds is you want someone who when they will listen, gather all the facts, take input from their team including us—but we're just one voice and a service provider ultimately—and then make decisive action, but also be willing to revisit it and carry it on. And so one of the signals that I think turns us off is when tension builds, people turn inward and they try to, they force themselves to come up with the answers and they shut off communication, they shut off input, etc. I think the best leaders are the ones who are brilliant, who are convicted, and yet they're reaching outbound when intensity mounts and they need more input and they seek it out, and then they make a more informed decision. And I think that's the mark of a mature, confident leader who's going to work through problems and wrong answers to get to the best answers. Why did you decide to sell the company? IBM was our biggest single partner. They were the right distribution platform and, back then, hundreds of millions of dollars was real money. It was the biggest enterprise software outcome of the vintage at the time. We need to remember how much things have changed. But they paid forward value and our team felt it was the right fit. And so we were profitable, we were global, we were starting to think about an IPO. Ironically, my first IPO as an investor, Cornerstone OnDemand, was actually around the same scale and they traded up to billions. And so, I've thought back about the playbook and the decision many times, but never regret a good outcome in the sense of not just financially but fit and team. That team, half that team still exists over there. I mean, our type A executives all left pretty quickly, but the team, the product, it's still in use years later and that's pretty cool. How long did you stay at IBM? One year and one day. Not that anyone's keeping track. I always like to overdeliver. I said, "What's the bare minimum where you will feel good about this?" and they said, "One year." And I said, "Okay," and I left—I told them, "I'll commit to my year." I stayed one extra day very purposely and then jumped out. What was that one year like for you? Tough. IBM is a great big company and a great big company and there were meeting after meeting after meeting that overlapped. I could have been meeting all day and interestingly, I could have ignored all the meetings and no one would have noticed because they would have assumed I was in another meeting. And that was just so painful for me as a type A person who's aggressive, who wants to get out there and do it. The idea of resting and vesting for me was just mind-numbing. And my senior execs felt the same way. And so, I loved IBM, I wanted them to be happy, I wanted this to carry through, but I needed out. Okay, so one year, one day hits, you walk out the door of IBM, what do you go do next? I went straight to Bessemer. So, I'd worked in venture before, not with Bessemer but with another great firm, TA Associates, back when they still did venture. And we were fortunate to have a number of A-tier firms around our cap table with Trigo, raised some great folks. Bessemer was our anchor investor but more importantly, the firm that I felt best about, their product, their service, their delivery. And I was fortunate to have a number of offers to go join various venture firms, and hands down Bessemer was my top pick. And you didn't want to go operate again? You didn't want to go build something all over again? I didn't at the point. One of the things that was intoxicating to me about venture was the potential to be involved early stage and see things and incubate things and yet satisfy my ADD and doing a bunch of things. I'll confess that I've done much less of the incubation and creation than I thought I would because I've been so excited by other people's ideas and other teams. And so, I've only done a little bit of that in my Bessemer time, and the vast majority of what I do is find great teams that are already executing better than I could have, would have, or would have found and begging my way in to work with them. What were some of the big early lessons when you went back to venture, when you went to Bessemer? Don't try to be the operator. And that manifests itself in a few ways, but one of them from the investing side is: don't find things that are doing okay and go in to fix them so that you go from good to great. Go find the teams and the business that's already great and make them excellent, or help them just stay excellent if they're already there so that the slope of line stays bigger. And that carries through the board interactions. Hippocratic oath of venture: do no harm. Like, stay the hell out of the way when things are working and send them customers, send them candidates, but like get out of the road if they're executing well and going. Don't try to give product input out of cycle or sitting in a board meeting. Like, there's a process and a way to do it, but back great teams and let them run and then just resource them. Yeah, there's like a double-edged sword to having venture investors who've been operators, right? How are you framing the value that you bring with that background as an operator and now that you're an investor? Yeah, so I think it's a balance which is, I mean, we have a large global firm: 20 billion in capital, eight offices around the world, over 100 people on our platform team to support our companies. So, we want to help, we want to engage, but the mentality and the approach is very different. Bessemer is a low-bravado firm. We're not putting our firm first, we're not out there pushing things. It's all "What do you want?" And so my interactions with my CEOs, which are almost daily with almost every one of them— Really? But it's sending quick texts, it's sending emails, it's forward things around, it's after-hours calls and obviously structured board meetings and things, but much more of it is the quick little hits. "Hey, saw this great candidate, think they're interesting for your VP of X role," or "Just met with this interesting partner, it's worth your time." And if it's a trusted relationship, eight out of 10 of those is essentially "no," which is "Thanks, not interested," or "Hey, could you buy some time or run interference or whatever?" And two of those are like, "Oh yeah, I want that immediately, send it over and make it happen." But that's if it's high frequency, low risk, and very trusted, then it's just high velocity. And my CEOs will say without a doubt I can be on the phone within 90 minutes when needed—I've been woken up countless times with chaos moments and in crisis. There are a few things I want to tease out of this. It's double opt-in, which I'm so grateful to hear. It's like you're not just saying like, "Hey, I'm connecting you with so-and-so," you're asking. Even if you might be pushing hard, "I think this one's really high value—this job candidate, this prospective client, whatever it might be." I don't think enough people fully appreciate the value of the double opt-in. And that leads directly into the second tease out, which is time is our most valuable asset and not everything has to be a 30-minute phone call. But that quick hit of like, "Hey, just flagging this for you, could be of interest, let me know, whatever." I mean, that is invaluable to an operator where every minute really counts in their day. I have a different modality of communication for most of my CEOs. Like I literally have WhatsApp, Slack, Signal, text, email, FaceTime by preference. I could tell you which CEO I communicate with in which way. I have blocks of time for fixed things: board meetings, new company discussions, conversations like this. But half my time is interrupt-driven and I'd say evenings and weekends are almost always interrupt-driven. And so I don't do a lot of long-form things. I don't sit down and read a book. I don't sit down and do long-duration things. Most of my interactions are prioritized kind of tier one and working through that. And for me, it's very much the back and forth. I'm constantly seeking out experts on every topic and having the discussion and going back and forth and then I'll attempt to synthesize that and then go back to the CEO or the team member and go through. And by the way, we are totally transparent with our diligence issues and concerns and we don't dance around. We'll literally share it after the investment, like "Here's the readout. Here's what we heard, here are the really red flags." I was on Capitol Hill early in my career and one of my mentors said to me before I started there, "Most Fridays your boss will go back to his state and a lot of your colleagues will take a three-hour lunch. And I'm just letting you know that by virtue of where you work, you can call almost anyone in the country and they'll want to talk to you. And the number one thing most people want to talk about more than anything else is themselves and they will think you are really smart if all you do is ask them questions." Right? And so I just, I love that you're in this position where you can call people who are true subject matter experts and give them the privilege of making you smarter on something that may well make you and your LPs a ton of money. And they feel great about being able to do that. And especially in these deep science fields where nuclear physicists and theorists have wanted to commercialize this technology for decades and are looking for avenues to do that. And so they want to talk—and by the way, there's also ways for them to hopefully make a lot of money in the process by... we'll bring people on as paid advisors in times and certainly our consultants were paid. But most scientists, most computer scientists, enter this because of love of tech and love of what could be and more than anything they want to see success and they want to see it happen and we're aligned there. Yeah, and I think we underestimate how much people want to help. And especially when they've spent years acquiring subject matter knowledge and they can now deploy it to someone who genuinely cares about what they're doing. And yes, if I can make money doing it, all the better. But sometimes asking is all you have to do to get what you need. Very right, and I think that's great advice to the listeners: to reach out. What were some of the most important lessons from your early days at Bessemer? The big swings, like the crimes of omission are the ones that hurt more. So, it's so easy to talk yourself out of investments. And unfortunately, just by its nature, we say no 99.9% of the time anyway, but there are so many ways things can fail and especially the most bold entrepreneurs and the most bold visions have a lot of ways to fail spectacularly. I keep pushing myself to think about the "what can be," what can go right, what can really work with this? And quite sincerely the things that I regret the most are the ones that I pulled back on because I could see the big vision and just saw all the probabilities of the "what can go wrong" and just couldn't quite get there and I regret those the most. And how has that informed how you're investing in this era? To start with the reverse and to think back on how could the world change to accept this and what's possible in a multi-horizon way. Great businesses often start with a line of sight to cash flow positive and controlling our own destiny—I think that's important. But the multi-horizon view is critical. And so if you look at a business like Shopify that my partner Jeremy Levine funded, or Procore or ServiceTitan or some of these vertical SaaS businesses, you could look at ServiceTitan and say that's software for plumbers, how big can that be, etc. If you step back and say it's actually multi-vertical, it's actually payments relevant, it's actually a great AI use case. They're at 10 billion already and sky's the limit in terms of where that can go. And then by the way, often the tiebreaker comes down to just bluntly, what teams do you want to work with and where can you change the world? We have our growth fund is called Century Fund because it's anchored on this idea of: what are going to be the iconic companies of the next century? Like when the books are written, our kids talk about it, who are the companies that dented the space-time continuum? And there's a lot of ways to make money in venture. It is a great asset class, it's a great time. So when I can only do two deals a year, the tiebreaker comes down to the biggest swings and the teams I really want to work with for a decade-plus. We just ran the math: our average hold period's like 14 years, longer even than I thought. These are long journeys if you're doing really big things. And so the qualitative does matter, too. Do you all have conviction that there are going to be great companies built now that will last a century? Or are you more looking on a 10- to 20-year horizon for where that value will be created and you don't worry about it beyond that? We don't think tech's going away. We don't think software's disappearing despite the current narrative. When you look at a Rocket Lab and space in our frontier tech side or the nuclear conversation we just had, or you look at Anthropic and the foundation models, or you look at a number of our vertical SaaS companies or infrastructure—MaintainX or ClickHouse or some of these—we absolutely believe these can all be $100 billion plus businesses and that they are the new foundation for the new economy. One of the things that you all are known for is the anti-portfolio. Could you share a little bit about how that came to be, what it is and how it came to be? Yes. So, on our website, in addition to the portfolio which we're very proud of— Which every venture firm has. Yeah, but I think the portfolio needs to look good to be able to do this. But we have a page which I'd encourage you all to go to, which is the anti-portfolio and it's all about those crimes of omission that I alluded to before. It's the missed deals. It's not the companies we invest in that fail—we have a lot of those, we don't want to single out entrepreneurs in that, and we want to continue to fail in that way because that means you're taking big risks. But these are the ones that ended up being great companies that we had a front-row seat and an opportunity to invest in and blew it. And those are the ones that are most important to learn from. And so, for me it's companies like Tesla early on and Atlassian on the software side, on and on like this where massive respect for the entrepreneur, belief in the vision, and just couldn't quite get there. And we need to keep asking ourselves in those ways, what did we miss and what can we learn so we don't make that mistake twice in a row? Because that hurts. And then part of the reason to keep it out there, not only to remind ourselves but also for those 99.9% of entrepreneurs that we say no to, it's like we're still rooting for you. Like we're fans of tech, we love innovation, we love that you're doing this. And like if you can't make it onto our portfolio page, which we certainly hope you do, we actually hope you make it onto the anti-portfolio and we learn from that. So that next time on your next company, hopefully, if you're nice enough to give us another shot or for the next entrepreneur like you, we say "yes" that time. I'd love to spend a minute on Anthropic. Could you tell the story of how you all made the decision to invest there? Yes, so we were very close to Cloud Wave 1, saw the hyperscaler market play out, which really wasn't a venture capital game obviously—the winners Amazon, Microsoft, Google. There could have been a case that one of the independent challengers emerged there, but there was no one that was willing to fund the capital for those on the private markets and we all missed it. We didn't want to miss that again. Our belief is that the foundation models are going to be the new hyperscalers. And we don't do competitive investments, so we could only make one bet. And so we were very much looking for: who do we think was going to pull ahead several years out? But the bet on Anthropic was that they would be one of those companies and that they had the chance to compete at the one slot, which we very much still think they do. And credit to co-investors there, Matt Murphy at Menlo and Ravi at Lightspeed and some folks that were coming into the same round and were great collaborators and thought partners. And there's less of that venture these days, but we would not have gotten there without the ability to actually lock arms with a syndicate there and have real discussions.
